The Opening 'Wa Yajibu': Waw, Wujub, and the Imperfect Tense
Al-Bajuri explains the waw, the meaning of wujub, and the use of the imperfect tense.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the grammatical and terminological nuances of the opening phrase 'wa yajibu' in Sanusi's text, including the function of the waw, the two meanings of wujub, and the use of the imperfect tense.

Core Idea
Every word in kalam carries layers—grammar, semantics, and theology.

This lesson unpacks the very first word of Sanusi's creed ('wa yajibu') and shows how al-Bajuri clarifies its grammatical function, the precise meaning of 'obligation' here, and why the imperfect tense is used. These details are not pedantic; they shape how we read the entire text.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its translation to get an overview of al-Bajuri's analysis.
Notice the structure: he first addresses the waw, then the meaning of wujub, then the imperfect tense.
2
Identify the waw's function
Al-Bajuri says the waw is for isti'naf (resumption), not for conjunction. Understand the reasoning: an insha' (command) cannot be conjoined with an ikhbar (statement).
3
Distinguish two meanings of wujub
The passage clarifies that 'wujub' here means legal obligation (reward/punishment), while later it means necessary existence when referring to God. Note how the same term has different theological applications.
4
Understand the imperfect tense
The imperfect tense (yajibu) indicates continuous renewal, which fits the ongoing obligation for each generation. But this continuity comes from context, not the tense's inherent meaning.
Al-Bajuri ends with 'fata-dabbar' (so reflect), urging careful thought.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(قوله: وَيَجِبُ... إلخ) الواو للاستئناف لا للعطف؛ لأن ما قبلها - أعني قوله: اعلم... إلخ) - إنشاء، وما بعدها - أعني قوله : (يجب... إلخ) - إخبار، ولا يُعْطَفُ أحدهما على الآخر على الصحيح.
وقد عُلِمَ مما مر أن المراد بالوجوب في مثل هذه العبارة بمعنى كَوْنِ الشيء بحيث يُثَابُ على فِعْلِه ويعاقب على تركه، بخلافه في قوله بعد:(فما يجب في حق مولانا ونحوه فإنه بمعنى عدم قبول الانتفاء.
وعَبَّر بالمضارع؛ لأنه يَدُلُّ على الاستمرار التجددي، وهو مناسب للمقام هنا؛ لأن وجودَ ذلك يتجدد بتجدُّد المكلفين وقتاً بعد وقت، لكن دلالة المضارع على ذلك ليست بالوضع، بل بالقرينة؛ لأنه موضوع للحَدَث في المستقبل أو في الحال؛ ولو مَرَّةً واحدةً. فتدبر.
His saying: 'And it is obligatory ... etc.' The waw is for isti'naf (resumption), not for conjunction; because what precedes it—I mean his saying: 'Know ... etc.'—is an insha' (performative utterance), and what follows it—I mean his saying: 'it is obligatory ... etc.'—is an ikhbar (informative statement), and one is not conjoined with the other according to the correct view. And it has been known from what preceded that what is meant by wujub in such an expression is the meaning of a thing being such that one is rewarded for doing it and punished for abandoning it, unlike its meaning in his later saying: 'for what is wājib with respect to our Master and the like,' for that means the impossibility of non-existence. And he expressed it with the imperfect tense because it indicates continuous renewal, and it is suitable for the context here because the existence of that is renewed with the renewal of the obligated individuals time after time, but the indication of the imperfect tense to that is not by linguistic convention, but by contextual evidence; because it is coined for an event in the future or in the present, even once. So reflect.
Source pages p. 76

Commentary
This passage is a grammatical and terminological analysis by al-Bajuri of the opening phrase 'wa yajibu' in Sanusi's text. He first addresses the waw: it is a waw isti'naf, not a waw atf, because the preceding utterance 'know...' is an insha' (a command) while the following 'it is obligatory...' is an informative statement, and the correct view is that these two types are not conjoined. Then he clarifies that the term 'wujub' in this context means that the act is such that one is rewarded for performing it and punished for omitting it—i.e., legal obligation on the servant. This is distinct from the meaning of wujub later in the text (with respect to God) where it means the impossibility of non-existence, i.e., a necessary attribute of God. Finally, he explains why the author used the imperfect tense (yajibu): it indicates continuous renewal, which fits the ongoing obligation for successive generations of the obligated. However, he notes that this continuous sense is not inherent in the imperfect tense linguistically—it is only by context (qarina) such as the phrase 'on every obligated person'—because the imperfect tense is originally for a single event in the future or present.

Key Terms
الواو للاستئناف
الإنشاء
الإخبار
الوجوب
الاستمرار التجددي
القرينة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That wujub always means legal obligation.
Correction: The passage shows that wujub can also mean necessary existence when referring to God.

Checkpoint
What does al-Bajuri say about the waw in 'wa yajibu'?
A) It is a waw atf (conjunction)
B) It is a waw isti'naf (resumption)
C) It is a waw for oath
D) It is a redundant particle

Key Takeaway
The opening phrase 'wa yajibu' is carefully chosen: the waw resets the discourse, wujub here means legal obligation, and the imperfect tense signals ongoing obligation through context.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Al-Bajuri shows that even the smallest word 'wa' carries a precise meaning. When you read the Qur'an, do you assume that every particle is deliberate? What might you be missing if you rush over the 'wa's and 'fa's?
?
The term 'wajib' shifts meaning depending on whether it refers to God or to humans. Have you ever confused what is necessary for God (like His existence) with what is required of you (like prayer)? How does this distinction deepen your understanding of tawhid?
?
The imperfect tense indicates ongoing obligation only by context. In your own life, is your sense of obligation to God renewed by the context of each day? Or have you let the urgency fade? What one 'wajib' have you been treating as if it were a single event rather than a continuous call?

Definition of the Mukallaf and Status of Jinn and Angels
Al-Bajuri defines the mukallaf and explains jinn and angels' status.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand who qualifies as a mukallaf (legally responsible person) and why jinn are obligated but angels are not.

Core Idea
Obligation requires adulthood, sanity, sound senses, and reaching of the call.

Al-Bajuri clarifies a key term from Sanusi's text. Before discussing what the mukallaf must believe, it is essential to know who qualifies as such. This passage defines the conditions of responsibility and distinguishes the cases of jinn and angels.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get an overview, then read the literal translation. Notice how Al-Bajuri begins by expanding on 'every mukallaf' to include jinn.
The passage is a definition with clarifications.
2
Identify the definition of mukallaf
Look for the sentence that begins 'واعلم أن المكلَّفَ'. It lists four conditions: adult, sane, sound of senses, and reached by the call. Notice that 'sound of senses' is minimal (hearing or sight).
These conditions are taken from the source.
3
Notice the exclusions
The passage explicitly excludes children (even discerning), the insane, those lacking senses, and those unreached by the call. Understand why each is excluded.
4
Understand the status of jinn and angels
The passage states jinn are obligated (from creation) but angels are not, based on the most correct view. The Prophet's sending to angels was one of honor, not obligation.
This is a key distinction.
5
Recognize the nuance about children's worship
Al-Bajuri explains that requiring worship from a discerning child is not obligation but encouragement to form a habit.
This avoids confusion about children's accountability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: عَلَى كُلّ مُكَلَّف أي كلّ فَرْدٍ فردٍ من أفراد المكلفين ولو من الجنّ؛ لأنهم مُكلفون كالإنس، لكن تكليفهم من حين الخلقة، وأما الملائكة؛ فليسوا بمكلفين على الراجح وإن كان النبي الله مُرْسَلاً إليهم؛ لأن إرساله إليهم إنما هو إرسال تشريف لا إرسال تكليف.
واعلم أن المكلَّفَ : هو البالغ العاقل، سليم الحواس - ولو السمع أو البصر فقط ، الذي بَلَغَتْهُ الدعوة. فخرج الصبي ولو مميزاً، والمجنون، وفاقد الحواس، ومَنْ لم تبلغه الدعوة، فليس كل منهم مكلَّفاً وطَلَبُ العبادة من الصبي المميز كالصلاة والصوم ليست لتكليفه بها، بل لترغيبه فيها ليعتادها، فلا يتركها إن شاء الله تعالى.
His saying: 'Upon every obligated person' means every single individual among the obligated individuals, even from the jinn; because they are obligated like humans, but their obligation is from the moment of creation. As for the angels, they are not obligated according to the most correct view, even though the Prophet ﷺ was sent to them; because his being sent to them is only a sending of honor, not a sending of obligation. And know that the obligated person is: the adult, sane, sound of senses—even if only hearing or sight—who has received the call. Thus excluded are the child, even if discerning, the insane, the one lacking senses, and the one who has not received the call; so not every one of them is obligated. And the request for worship from the discerning child, like prayer and fasting, is not to obligate him thereby, but to encourage him to do it so that he becomes accustomed to it, so that he does not abandon it, God willing.
Source pages p. 77

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'mukallaf' (the legally responsible person) as used in Sanusi's text. Al-Bajuri first explains that 'every obligated person' includes even the jinn, as they are obligated like humans, but their obligation begins at their creation. In contrast, angels are not obligated on the predominant view, even though the Prophet was sent to them; that sending is one of honor, not of imposing duties. Then he gives the precise definition of a mukallaf: an adult, sane person with sound senses (at least hearing or sight) who has received the prophetic call. This definition excludes children (even discerning ones), the insane, those lacking senses, and those unreached by the call. He adds that the requirement of worship from a discerning child is not truly obligation but encouragement to habituate them to worship.

Key Terms
مكلَّف
الجن
الملائكة
تكليف
إرسال تشريف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think angels are obligated like humans.
Correction: Angels are not obligated on the correct view; their worship is innate and the Prophet's sending to them was one of honor.

Checkpoint
What is the status of angels regarding taklif according to al-Bajuri?
A) They are obligated like humans
B) They are obligated but from creation
C) They are not obligated on the correct view
D) They are obligated only for certain commands

Key Takeaway
The definition of mukallaf requires adulthood, sanity, sound senses, and receiving the call; jinn are included, but angels are not obligated.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You are an adult, sane, with sound senses, and the call has reached you. How many of your daily actions are done with the awareness that you are a mukallaf, accountable to Allah? What specific habit can you change today to live more consciously as one who is obligated?
?
You have a discerning child or know one. Are you training them in worship out of mere habit, or are you consciously encouraging them so that obligation becomes natural? What will you do this week to make worship attractive to them without pressure?
?
The jinn are obligated from their creation, yet you have been given time. If you were to die tomorrow, would your obligation be fulfilled? What is one obligation you have been neglecting that you will commit to starting today?

Three Schools on Judgments: Ash'ari, Maturidi, Mu'tazila
Sanusi enumerates the three theological schools and their views on the source of judgments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three major theological schools' positions on the source of moral and religious judgments.

Core Idea
The three schools differ on whether reason or revelation establishes judgments.

Sanusi summarizes the three famous Muslim theological schools—Ash'ari, Maturidi, and Mu'tazila—regarding how judgments like obligation and prohibition are determined. This classification is foundational for understanding Islamic theology's debates on ethics and law. The passage clearly distinguishes each school's stance on the roles of reason and divine revelation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the three schools
Read the Arabic text and its translation carefully. Notice that Sanusi presents three positions concisely.
The passage begins with 'فتحصل أن المذاهب ثلاثة' – 'It is concluded that the schools are three.'
2
Identify each school's position
For each school, note what it says about the basis of judgments (shar'/’aql) and any conditions.
Ash'aris: all judgments by shar' but conditioned on reason. Maturidis: distinguish knowledge obligation from others. Mu'tazila: all by reason based on rational good/evil.
3
Reflect on the word 'تأمل'
Sanusi ends with 'فتأمل' (so reflect). Consider why he asks for reflection and what the key difference is.
This invites the student to ponder the implications of each position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فتحصل أن المذاهب ثلاثة الأول: مذهب الأشاعرة: وهو أن الأحكام كلها تثبت بالشرع لكن بشرط العقل والثاني مذهب الماتريدية: وهو التفصيل بين وجوب المعرفة وبين سائر الأحكام. والثالثُ مذهبُ المعتزلة: وهو أن الأحكام كلها ثبتت بالعقل بناءً على التحسين والتقبيح العقليين. فتأمل.

(قوله: بِنَاءً عَلَى التَّحْسِينِ... إلخ) فما أدرك العقل مفسدةً في فعله كالظلم فهو الحرام، وما أدرك مفسدة في تركه فهو الواجب كالعدل، وما أدرك مصلحة في فعله ولم يدرك مفسدة في تركه فهو المندوب وما أدرك مصلحة في تركه ولم يدرك مفسدة في فعله فهو المكروه، وما لم يدرك مصلحة في فعله ولا تركه ولا مفسدة فيهما فهو المباح. اهـ ملخصاً من 'حاشية الهدهدي'.
It is concluded that the schools (madhaahib) are three: The first is the school of the Ash'aris: that all judgments are established by the Revealed Law (shar') but on the condition of reason. The second is the school of the Maturidis: that there is a distinction between the obligation of knowledge and the rest of the judgments. The third is the school of the Mu'tazila: that all judgments are established by reason, based on intellectual approval and disapproval (al-tahseen wa al-taqbeeh al-'aqliyyayn). So reflect.

(His saying: 'Based on approval... etc.') Whatever reason perceives as harm in its action, like injustice, is forbidden (haraam); whatever it perceives as harm in its omission, it is obligatory (waajib), like justice; whatever it perceives as benefit in its action and does not perceive harm in its omission, it is recommended (mandub); whatever it perceives as benefit in its omission and does not perceive harm in its action, it is disliked (makruh); and whatever it perceives neither benefit in its action nor its omission, nor harm in either, it is permissible (mubaah). End, summarized from the Haashiyat al-Hudhudi.
Source pages p. 80

Commentary
This passage is a classification of theological schools (madhahib) regarding how moral and religious judgments are established. The author states that there are three positions: (1) The Ash'aris hold that all judgments are grounded in revelation but that reason is a prerequisite for receiving revelation. (2) The Maturidis distinguish between the obligation to know God (which reason can establish) and other judgments (which come from revelation). (3) The Mu'tazila hold that all judgments are established by reason alone, based on the rational principles of good and evil (tahseen and taqbeeh). The author then provides an explanatory note (hashiya) detailing the Mu'tazili taxonomy of actions: if reason perceives harm in doing an act, it is forbidden; harm in omitting it makes it obligatory; benefit in doing it without harm in omitting makes it recommended; benefit in omitting it without harm in doing makes it disliked; and when no benefit or harm is perceived, the act is permissible. This note, summarized from the commentary of al-Hudhudi, clarifies the Mu'tazili ethical framework.

Key Terms
المذاهب
الأشاعرة
الماتريدية
المعتزلة
الأحكام
الشرع
العقل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Ash'ari position completely denies reason any role.
Correction: The Ash'aris require reason as a condition (bishart al-‘aql) for revelation to be valid, so reason plays a crucial preparatory role.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which school holds that all judgments are established by reason alone?
A) Ash'aris
B) Maturidis
C) Mu'tazila
D) All of the above

Key Takeaway
The three schools—Ash'ari, Maturidi, and Mu'tazila—differ fundamentally on whether judgments are sourced in revelation or reason.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a moral dilemma, which of these three positions do you actually rely on in your heart—revelation, reason, or a mix? Does your daily practice match what you claim to believe?
?
If reason alone cannot determine good and evil, as the Ash'aris hold, then what does that mean for your own judgments when revelation is silent? Are you willing to submit your reasoning to divine command even when it feels uncomfortable?
?
The Mu'tazila trust their intellect to discern right from wrong. How confident are you in your own intellect to independently arrive at truth without divine guidance? Have you ever been certain about something, only to later discover it was wrong?

Mu'tazili Classification of Actions: The Five Rulings
The hashiva explains the Mu'tazili classification of actions into five categories based on rational ethics.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazili ethical taxonomy of actions based on rational perception of benefit and harm.

Core Idea
For the Mu'tazila, reason alone can classify actions into five categories: obligatory, forbidden, recommended, disliked, and permissible.

The Mu'tazila developed a detailed system of ethical classification based on reason's perception of benefit (maslaha) and harm (mafsada). This passage, summarized from al-Hudhudi's commentary, lists the five categories: haram, wajib, mandub, makruh, and mubah. Each is defined by whether the action or its omission involves benefit or harm as perceived by reason.

Guided Path
1
Read the explanatory note
Read the Arabic text and translation of the hashiva. Note that it begins with 'binaa’ 'ala al-tahseen...' and describes five categories.
The text uses 'idraka' (perceives) and 'mafsada' (harm) and 'maslaha' (benefit).
2
Identify each of the five categories
Match each definition (harm in doing/omitting, benefit in doing/omitting) to the correct ruling: haram, wajib, mandub, makruh, mubah.
The definitions are paired: harm in action → haram; harm in omission → wajib; benefit in action, no harm in omission → mandub; benefit in omission, no harm in action → makruh; no benefit or harm → mubah.
3
Reflect on the rational basis
Consider how this classification relies solely on reason's ability to discern good and evil without revelation.
This is part of the Mu'tazili doctrine of tahseen wa taqbeeh 'aqliyyan.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فتحصل أن المذاهب ثلاثة الأول: مذهب الأشاعرة: وهو أن الأحكام كلها تثبت بالشرع لكن بشرط العقل والثاني مذهب الماتريدية: وهو التفصيل بين وجوب المعرفة وبين سائر الأحكام. والثالثُ مذهبُ المعتزلة: وهو أن الأحكام كلها ثبتت بالعقل بناءً على التحسين والتقبيح العقليين. فتأمل.

(قوله: بِنَاءً عَلَى التَّحْسِينِ... إلخ) فما أدرك العقل مفسدةً في فعله كالظلم فهو الحرام، وما أدرك مفسدة في تركه فهو الواجب كالعدل، وما أدرك مصلحة في فعله ولم يدرك مفسدة في تركه فهو المندوب وما أدرك مصلحة في تركه ولم يدرك مفسدة في فعله فهو المكروه، وما لم يدرك مصلحة في فعله ولا تركه ولا مفسدة فيهما فهو المباح. اهـ ملخصاً من 'حاشية الهدهدي'.
It is concluded that the schools (madhaahib) are three: The first is the school of the Ash'aris: that all judgments are established by the Revealed Law (shar') but on the condition of reason. The second is the school of the Maturidis: that there is a distinction between the obligation of knowledge and the rest of the judgments. The third is the school of the Mu'tazila: that all judgments are established by reason, based on intellectual approval and disapproval (al-tahseen wa al-taqbeeh al-'aqliyyayn). So reflect.

(His saying: 'Based on approval... etc.') Whatever reason perceives as harm in its action, like injustice, is forbidden (haraam); whatever it perceives as harm in its omission, it is obligatory (waajib), like justice; whatever it perceives as benefit in its action and does not perceive harm in its omission, it is recommended (mandub); whatever it perceives as benefit in its omission and does not perceive harm in its action, it is disliked (makruh); and whatever it perceives neither benefit in its action nor its omission, nor harm in either, it is permissible (mubaah). End, summarized from the Haashiyat al-Hudhudi.
Source pages p. 80

Commentary
This passage is a classification of theological schools (madhahib) regarding how moral and religious judgments are established. The author states that there are three positions: (1) The Ash'aris hold that all judgments are grounded in revelation but that reason is a prerequisite for receiving revelation. (2) The Maturidis distinguish between the obligation to know God (which reason can establish) and other judgments (which come from revelation). (3) The Mu'tazila hold that all judgments are established by reason alone, based on the rational principles of good and evil (tahseen and taqbeeh). The author then provides an explanatory note (hashiya) detailing the Mu'tazili taxonomy of actions: if reason perceives harm in doing an act, it is forbidden; harm in omitting it makes it obligatory; benefit in doing it without harm in omitting makes it recommended; benefit in omitting it without harm in doing makes it disliked; and when no benefit or harm is perceived, the act is permissible. This note, summarized from the commentary of al-Hudhudi, clarifies the Mu'tazili ethical framework.

Key Terms
التحسين والتقبيح العقليين
الحرام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Mu'tazila reject all use of revelation for ethics.
Correction: The Mu'tazila hold that reason alone can determine ethical value, but revelation still provides additional guidance; this passage focuses on the rational basis.

Checkpoint
According to the Mu'tazili classification in this passage, what makes an action obligatory (wajib)?
A) Benefit in performing it
B) Harm in omitting it
C) Harm in performing it
D) No benefit or harm

Key Takeaway
For the Mu'tazila, reason's perception of benefit and harm in actions and their omissions yields five ethical categories.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If reason alone can determine what is obligatory and forbidden, what injustice have you committed or allowed yourself to ignore because you thought it wasn't 'harmful enough'? Will you correct it today?
?
The passage says that reason perceives benefit in some acts (mandub) but does not require them. What act of charity or goodness have you been postponing, knowing it is beneficial but thinking it is merely optional? Why do you treat it as optional when your heart knows it is needed?
?
You have just read that reason can classify actions without revelation. How often do you seek fatwas from scholars for things your own conscience already condemns? Are you hiding behind religious authority to avoid the call of your own God-given reason?

Rulings on Nazar (Speculation) and Their Basis
Sanusi discusses the legal status of nazar and its variant rulings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the differing legal rulings on nazar (speculative reasoning) and how the preponderant view conditions its obligation on the ability to perform it.

Core Idea
The obligation of nazar depends on one's ability to perform it.

This passage from Sanusi addresses a fundamental methodological question: is speculative reasoning about creed obligatory, and if so, with what force? The text presents three views and settles on a nuanced position that ties obligation to capability. Let's explore the views and the reasoning behind the preferred opinion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text and the translation. Notice the structure: first the statement that the disagreement is based on a prior disagreement about nazar, then the three views, then the preponderant view. The commentary clarifies key terms.
The passage includes both the matn and a hashiya commentary.
2
Identify the three views
List the three views: (1) nazar is obligatory as usool (abandonment leads to disbelief), (2) obligatory as furu' (abandonment is sinful), (3) recommended but still earns reward of an obligatory act.
3
Understand the preponderant view
Examine the preponderant view: nazar is obligatory as furu' for those who are able to perform it, and not obligatory for those who are unable. Note the condition of ability.
This reconciles the views by making obligation contingent on capacity.
4
Review the commentary's clarifications
The commentary explains that 'wujub al-asl' means negligence entails kufr, 'wujub al-furu'' entails sin like abandoning prayer, and 'mandub' still yields reward of an obligatory act.
This helps distinguish the severity of each ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهذا الخلافُ مبني على الخلاف في النظر؛ فقيل: إنه واجب وجوب الأصول مطلقاً. وقيل: إنه واجبٌ وجوب الفروع كذلك. وقيل: إنه مندوب كذلك أيضاً. والراجح أنه واجبٌ وجوب الفروع إن كان فيه قُدْرَةٌ عليه، وغيرُ واجب إن لم يَكُنْ فيه تلك القدرة. فتدبر.

(قوله: وَاجِب وُجُوبَ الْأَصْلِ بمعنى أن تاركه كافر، فوجوبه كوجوب الجزم بالعقائد في أن تركه يقتضي الكفر.
(قوله: إِنَّهُ وَاجِبٌ وُجُوبَ الْفُرُوع بمعنى أن تاركه يكون عاصياً كتارك الصلاة والصوم.
(قوله: وَقِيلَ: إِنَّهُ مَنْدُوبٌ ومع ذلك إذا فعله يثاب عليه ثواب الواجب كما في حاشية الهدهدي" نقلاً عن يس.
This disagreement is based on the disagreement concerning speculation (nazar); for it is said: it is obligatory with the obligation of [matters of] principle (usool) absolutely; and it is said: it is obligatory with the obligation of [matters of] branches (furu') likewise; and it is said: it is recommended (mandub) likewise also. The preponderant view is that it is obligatory with the obligation of branches if one has the ability to perform it, and not obligatory if one does not have that ability. So ponder.

(His saying: 'Obligatory with the obligation of principle' means that the one who abandons it is a disbeliever, so its obligation is like the obligation of decisive belief in the creedal matters in that abandoning it necessitates disbelief. (His saying: 'It is obligatory with the obligation of branches' means that the one who abandons it is a sinner, like the one who abandons prayer and fasting. (His saying: 'It is said: it is recommended' and nevertheless if he does it he is rewarded with the reward of an obligatory act, as in the Haashiyat al-Hudhudi, quoting from Yass.
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This passage addresses the legal ruling on nazar (speculative reasoning or inquiry) in matters of creed. It begins by stating that the differing views on the obligation of nazar derive from a prior disagreement about the nature of nazar itself. Three positions are reported: (1) nazar is obligatory with the same force as principles of faith (usool)—i.e., abandoning it entails disbelief; (2) it is obligatory with the force of branch rulings (furu')—i.e., abandoning it is sinful but not disbelief; and (3) it is merely recommended (mandub). The author then gives the preponderant view: nazar is obligatory (like branches) only for those who are capable of performing it; for those incapable, it is not obligatory. The accompanying commentary (hashiya) clarifies each term: 'wujub al-asl' means that neglect constitutes disbelief, similar to neglecting the fundamental creedal affirmations; 'wujub al-furu'' means that neglect is sinful, like omitting an obligatory prayer or fast; and if it is considered mandub, the one who performs it still receives the reward of an obligatory act—a nuance taken from al-Hudhudi.

الخلاف
وجوب الأصول
وجوب الفروع
المندوب
القدرة
الكفر
العصيان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think nazar is unconditionally obligatory for everyone regardless of capacity.
Correction: The passage clearly states that the preponderant view makes obligation conditional on ability: 'if one has the ability, obligatory; if not, not obligatory.'

Checkpoint
What is the preponderant view on the obligation of nazar according to the passage?
A) Obligatory as a principle (usool) absolutely
B) Obligatory as a branch (furu') for those able, not obligatory for those unable
C) Obligatory unconditionally
D) Merely recommended

Key Takeaway
The ruling on nazar is not absolute but conditioned on the ability to perform it, reconciling strict and lenient positions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have the ability to reason and reflect on your faith—when did you last truly exercise that ability to strengthen your conviction, or have you been coasting on inherited belief? What is stopping you from engaging in nazar right now?
?
If the obligation of nazar depends on your capacity, are you honest with yourself about your capacity? Are you making excuses of inability when you could actually dig deeper? Confront that today.
?
The preponderant view says nazar is obligatory for those who can. If you neglect it, consider that you may be sinning by omission. Will you schedule time this week to seriously study a creedal issue that you are unsure about, using the method of nazar?

Obligatory Knowledge: Detail vs. Summary
Sanusi explains the obligation of knowing divine matters in detail or in summary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the obligation to know divine attributes is graded: twenty must be known in detail, while the rest can be known in summary.

Core Idea
Not all divine knowledge is equal: some must be detailed, some suffices in summary.

Sanusi explains that the word 'ma' in 'what is obligatory' is general, but the actual obligation is specified. Rational and transmitted evidences establish some attributes in detail—namely the twenty essential attributes—and others only in summary. This passage clarifies that the mukallaf's duty varies accordingly.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its English translation. Notice the technical terms like tafseelan and ijmaalan.
The passage is from Sanusi's commentary on Umm al-Barahin.
2
Identify the two categories
The passage distinguishes between what must be known in detail (tafseelan) and what can be known in summary (ijmaalan). Find the definitions for each.
The twenty attributes are the detailed category.
3
Understand the role of evidence
Both categories are supported by rational or transmitted evidences. Notice that the difference is not the evidence but the depth of knowledge required.
4
Apply the principle to impossibility
The same rule applies to attributes impossible for God: some must be known in detail, others in summary.
This completes the symmetry of the principle.
5
Ponder the wisdom
Reflect on why this gradation exists. It balances the need for precise belief with the limit of human capacity.
Sanusi says 'fata'ammal' (so ponder).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: مَا يَجِبُ... إلخ) أي: جميع ما يجب إلخ؛ لأن (ما) من صيغ العموم، لكن ما قامت الأدلة العقلية أو النقلية عليه تفصيلاً - وهو العشرون الآتية -؛ يَجِبُ على المكلف أن يعرفه كذلك ـ أعني: تفصيلاً ـ، وما قامت الأدلة العقلية أو النقلية عليه إجمالاً ـ وهو سائر الكمالات - ؛ يجب على المكلف أن يعرفه كذلك ـ أعني: إجمالاً -، وكذلك يُقَال فيما يستحيلُ. فتأمل.
His saying: 'What is obligatory... etc.' i.e., all that is obligatory, etc.; because 'ma' is among the expressions of generality. However, what rational or transmitted evidences establish in detail (tafseelan)—and that is the coming twenty [attributes]—it is obligatory upon the legally responsible person (mukallaf) to know it likewise, i.e., in detail; and what rational or transmitted evidences establish in summary (ijmaalan)—and that is the rest of the perfections—it is obligatory upon the legally responsible person to know it likewise, i.e., in summary. And similarly it is said concerning what is impossible. So ponder.
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This passage explains the scope of obligatory knowledge for the mukallaf (legally responsible person). The author begins by interpreting the word 'ma' (what) in the phrase 'ma yajib' as a general term encompassing all that is obligatory to know about God. However, this general obligation is specified: (1) Those matters that are proven by rational or transmitted evidences in detail—namely, the twenty essential attributes that will be listed later—must be known in detail. (2) The remaining divine perfections, which are established only in summary form by the evidences, must be known in summary. The same principle applies to what is impossible for God (mustahil). Thus, the obligation is graded: the core creedal matters require detailed knowledge, while other attributes can be known in general.

Correction: The text clarifies only twenty require detail; the rest can be known in summary.

Key Takeaway
The mukallaf must know the twenty essential attributes in detail, while other divine perfections are known in summary.

?
Ponder the phrase 'so ponder' at the end. Have you taken time to reflect on the wisdom of this graduated obligation? Write down one insight that moves you to action.

Impossible and Permissible: Detailed vs. Summary Knowledge
Al-Bajuri defines mustahil and ja'iz for God and differentiates levels of knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between detailed knowledge (tafsīlan) and summary knowledge (ijmālan) required for what is impossible and permissible for God.

Core Idea
Knowing what is impossible for God requires both detail and summary—detail for the twenty opposites, summary for all other deficiencies.

Al-Bajuri clarifies an important principle in Islamic theology: not all that is impossible for God must be known in equal detail. While the twenty opposite attributes (like ignorance, weakness) must be known specifically, all other imperfections can be known in a general sense. This passage establishes the method of learning divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow, then read the English translation. Notice the technical terms and the structure.
The opening phrase about 'emptying before adorning' is a methodological note.
2
Identify the two categories of knowledge
Locate where Al-Bajuri distinguishes between detailed knowledge (tafsīlan) and summary knowledge (ijmālan) for the impossible attributes. Note what each category includes.
The twenty opposites are the detailed group; the rest of the deficiencies are the summary group.
3
Reflect on the reason for the distinction
Ask yourself why only the twenty opposites require detailed knowledge. Consider the role of evidence (adillah) in determining the level of knowledge.
Rational and transmitted proofs establish the twenty opposites in detail.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
من باب تقديم التخلية على التحلية. وقيل غير ذلك. (قوله: وَمَا يَسْتَحِيلُ) أي: في حق مولانا جل وعز، وكذلك يقال في قوله: (وما يجوز، ففيه الحذفُ من غير الأول لدلالته عليه، وقد علمت أن المراد: جميع ما يستحيل؛ لأن (ما) من صيغ العموم، لكن ما قامت الأدلة العقلية أو النقلية عليه تفصيلاً - وهو العشرون الأضداد الآتيةُ ؛ يجب على المكلَّف أن يعرفَهُ كذلك - أعني: تفصيلاً ، وما قامت الأدلة العقلية أو النقلية عليه إجمالاً - وهو سائر النقائص -؛ يجب المكلَّف أن يعرفه كذلك - أعني : إجمالاً ـ كما تقدم التنبيه عليه. (قوله: وَمَا يَجُوزُ أي: في حق مولانا جل وعز كما علمت
From the chapter of giving priority to emptying before adorning. And it is said otherwise. (His saying: 'And what is impossible') means: with respect to our Master, Mighty and Majestic, and likewise it is said in His saying: ('and what is permissible'), and in it there is deletion from the first due to its indication upon it. And you have known that the intended meaning is: all that is impossible; because 'what' is from the formulas of generality. But what rational or transmitted proofs establish in detail—and it is the twenty opposites coming—it is obligatory upon the obligated person to know it likewise—I mean: in detail. And what rational or transmitted proofs establish in summary—and it is the remaining deficiencies—it is obligatory upon the obligated person to know it likewise—I mean: in summary—as has been previously pointed out. (His saying: 'And what is permissible') means: with respect to our Master, Mighty and Majestic, as you have learned.
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and classificatory passage. Al-Bajuri begins with a phrase about giving priority to emptying before adorning, which is a methodological principle. Then he explains the terms 'what is impossible' (mustahil) and 'what is permissible' (ja'iz) as they apply to God. He notes that the word 'what' (ma) is general, covering all impossibilities. However, the required knowledge is differentiated: what is proven by rational or transmitted evidence in detail—namely, the twenty opposites (the attributes that are impossible for God, opposite to the necessary attributes)—must be known in detail by the obligated person. The remaining deficiencies, proven in summary, must be known in summary. This distinction between detailed and summary knowledge is crucial for proper theological understanding. The passage also clarifies that the same applies to 'what is permissible' (ja'iz).

Misconception: Thinking that all impossible attributes must be known in equal detail.
Correction: Al-Bajuri clarifies that only the twenty opposites (eternal attributes negated) require detailed knowledge; the rest of the deficiencies are known in summary.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway

?

?

The Amr I'tibari Is Stable for Attributes
Sanusi distinguishes amr i'tibari as stable for attributes, not the essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the 'amr i'tibari' is a conceptual entity that is stable for attributes rather than the essence, and how this distinction resolves an apparent objection.

Core Idea
The amr i'tibari relates to attributes, not the essence itself.

In this brief but precise passage, Sanusi clarifies a key point about al-amr al-iʿtibārī (the consideration). He explains that such conceptual entities attach to attributes, not directly to the essence, and gives examples to illustrate. He then addresses a potential objection about existence being a consideration that seems to attach to the essence, showing that the objection only holds if one affirms states (aḥwāl).

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Sanusi uses a very compressed style here.
2
Identify the rule about amr i'tibari
The passage begins with a clear statement: 'والأمر الاعتباري قار للصفات' meaning 'the consideration is stable for attributes'. Recognize this as the central thesis.
3
Examine the examples and comparison
Sanusi gives examples of power (qudra) and whiteness (bayāḍ) to illustrate that the subsistence (qiyām) of an attribute by the essence describes the attribute, not the essence. He then compares: what is stable for the essence is stronger.
This comparison sets up the objection.
4
Understand the objection and response
An objection claims that existence (wujūd) is a consideration yet stable for the essence. Sanusi replies that this distinction only holds if one affirms states (aḥwāl). Understand that the objection is not universally valid.
The reference to 'Sa'd' (Taftazani) is a tradition of kalam discourse.
5
Connect to the broader theory of attributes
Reflect on why this distinction matters: it preserves the transcendence of the divine essence by attributing conceptual entities to attributes rather than the essence itself, maintaining that attributes are not identical to the essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والأمر الاعتباري قار للصفات، فإن قيام القدرة بالذات الأقدس وصف للقدرة، وقيام البياض بزيدٍ وصف للبياض، وما كان قاراً للذات أقوى مما كان قاراً للصفة، ولا يرد ومن تبعه جعلوا الوجود أمراً اعتبارياً مع أنه قار للذات؛ لأن التفرقة على هذا: أن السعد المذكورة إنما هي على القول بإثبات الأحوال.
And the consideration (al-amr al-iʿtibārī) is stable (qārrun) for attributes. For the subsistence (qiyām) of power (al-qudra) by the most holy Essence (al-dhāt al-aqdas) is a description of power, and the subsistence of whiteness by Zayd is a description of whiteness. And that which is stable for the essence is stronger than that which is stable for the attribute. And it is not objected that [some] and those who followed them made existence (al-wujūd) a consideration while it is stable for the essence; because the distinction (al-tafriqa) in this regard is that [what was mentioned] of Saʿd is only upon the view affirming states (ithbāt al-aḥwāl).
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage defines an important distinction in the theory of attributes. The author establishes that the 'amr i'tibari' (a conceptual or consideration-dependent entity) is stable (qarar) for attributes, not for the essence. He gives two examples: the subsistence of God's power by the Essence is a description of the power, not the Essence; similarly, the subsistence of whiteness in Zayd is a description of the whiteness. That which is stable for the essence (like existence) is stronger. An objection is raised: some scholars consider existence a consideration even though it is stable for the essence. The author replies that this objection only applies if one affirms states (ahwal); the distinction is valid only on the view that affirms states.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the amr i'tibari being stable for attributes implies it is not real at all; rather, its stability is relative.
Correction: The passage shows that amr i'tibari is real in the sense that it describes attributes, but its stability is not absolute like that of the essence. The objection about existence shows that even existence, when considered a consideration, can be stable for the essence, but this is only under a specific ontological view (affirming states).

Checkpoint

B) It is stable for attributes.
C) It is equally stable for both.
D) It is never stable.

Key Takeaway
The amr i'tibari is a conceptual entity that is stable for attributes, not the essence, and this distinction is preserved even against the objection that existence is both a consideration and essence-stable.

How would your self-understanding change if you saw it as merely a description? Commit to remembering that your essence is deeper than any single quality.
?
Sanusi defends a subtle distinction with precise reasoning. Are you willing to put that same effort into understanding your beliefs about Allah? Reflect on a time when you accepted a simplified explanation of a theological matter without digging deeper. What is one theological concept you have taken for granted? Resolve to study it at a deeper level, starting with the distinctions made in this lesson.
?
The objection about existence is only valid under a specific view (affirming states). Have you ever rejected a truth because you misunderstood its conditions? Think of a belief or practice you dismissed without considering its context. How might that change if you applied the same careful distinction? Write down one thing you will re-examine with more precision.

Two Views on the Reality of Amr I'tibari
Al-Sanusi presents two opinions on whether amr i'tibari has real existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two competing views on whether amr i'tibari has real existence independent of the mind, and how the distinction between extractive and inventive considerations relates to this debate.

Core Idea
Amr i'tibari: some are real in themselves, others purely mental—but not all agree.

In Islamic theology, certain concepts like 'power subsisting in God's essence' are known as 'consideration-dependent entities' (amr i'tibari). The key question is whether such entities have any reality outside the mind. Al-Sanusi presents two positions, then clarifies that the answer depends on whether the consideration is extractive or inventive. This passage lays the groundwork for understanding how theologians evaluate the ontological status of relational attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Pay attention to the two views and the examples given.
Notice that the passage begins by mentioning Sa'd al-Taftazani's stance on states (ahwal).
2
Identify the two views
The first view holds that amr i'tibari has subsistence in itself disregarding the mind. The second view says it has no subsistence except in the mind. See which view the author favors.
The author indicates the first view is 'the apparent view that intellects are satisfied with.'
3
Understand the extractive vs. inventive distinction
The passage states that the two views apply to extractive considerations (like power subsisting in the essence). For inventive considerations (like a sea of mercury), all agree they are purely mental.
Extractive considerations are derived from actual relations; inventive are purely imagined.
4
Connect to the broader issue of states
Recall that Sa'd and his followers denied the reality of states (ahwal). This context explains why they might lean toward the purely mental view for certain considerations.
5
Reflect on al-Sharqawi's varying positions
Note that al-Sharqawi sometimes adopts one view and sometimes the other. This shows the complexity and ongoing debate among scholars.
His inconsistency highlights the subtlety of the issue.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والظاهر أن السعد ومن تبعه لا يقولون بثبوت الأحوال، وما ذكر من أن الأمر الاعتباري له ثبوت في نفسه بقطع النظر عن الذهن أحد قولين، وهو الظاهرُ الذي ترضاه العقول، وثانيهما: أنه لا ثبوت له إلا في الذهن، فالثابت في الواقع ذات الله تعالى وقدرته، وأما قيامها بالذات؛ فأمر ذهني فقط لا تحقق له في الواقع. وكلامُ الشيخ الشرقاوي في حاشية الهدهدي مختلف فتارةً قرّرَ أن الأمر الاعتباري ذهني فقط، ومثله بقيام قدرته بذاته تعالى، وتارة قرّر أن له حصولاً في نفسه بقطع النظر عن الذهن وهما قولان في الأمر الاعتباري الانتزاعي كقيام القدرة بالذات وقيام البياض بزيد، أما الاعتباري الاختراعي كبحر من زئبق؛ فهو ذهني اتفاقاً.
And it is apparent that Saʿd and those who followed him do not affirm the subsistence of states (thubūt al-aḥwāl). And what was mentioned that the consideration (al-amr al-iʿtibārī) has subsistence (thubūt) in itself, disregarding the mind, is one of two views—and it is the apparent view that intellects are satisfied with—and the second of them: that it has no subsistence except in the mind. So that which is subsistent in reality is the essence (dhāt) of God Most High and His power. As for its subsistence by the essence, it is only a mental matter (amr dhihnī) with no actualization in reality. And the statement of al-Shaykh al-Sharqāwī in his gloss on al-Hadiddī is varied: at times he established that the consideration is purely mental, and exemplified it by the subsistence of His power by His Essence Most High; and at times he established that it has occurrence (ḥuṣūl) in itself disregarding the mind. And these are two views concerning the extractive consideration (al-iʿtibārī al-intizāʿī), like the subsistence of power by the essence and the subsistence of whiteness by Zayd. As for the inventive consideration (al-iʿtibārī al-ikhtirāʿī), like a sea of mercury, it is purely mental by agreement.
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage explains the two main views on the nature of the 'amr i'tibari' (consideration-dependent entity). First, it notes that Sa'd al-Taftazani and his followers do not affirm the reality of states (ahwal). The author then presents two views: (1) the amr i'tibari has subsistence in itself independent of the mind, which is the more rational and apparent view; (2) it has no subsistence except in the mind, meaning that only God's essence and power are real in the external world, and the subsistence of power by the essence is purely mental. The passage then examines al-Sharqawi's commentary, noting that he sometimes adopts the purely mental view and sometimes the view that it has its own reality. Importantly, this applies specifically to the 'extractive consideration' (i'tibari intiza'i), which is derived from external relations like power subsisting in the essence or whiteness in Zayd. In contrast, the 'inventive consideration' (i'tibari ikhtira'i), such as a sea of mercury, is agreed to be purely mental.

Key Terms
الأمر الاعتباري (al-amr al-iʿtibārī)
الذهني (al-dhihnī)
الانتزاعي (al-intizāʿī)
الاختراعي (al-ikhtirāʿī)
الأحوال (al-aḥwāl)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All amr i'tibari are purely mental with no distinction.
Correction: The passage shows that extractive considerations may have subsistence in themselves (according to one view), while inventive considerations are purely mental by agreement.

Checkpoint
Which type of amr i'tibari is agreed to be purely mental?
A) The extractive (al-intizāʿī)
B) The inventive (al-ikhtirāʿī)
C) Both types
D) Neither type

Key Takeaway
The reality of amr i'tibari depends on whether it is extractive (possibly real) or inventive (purely mental), with scholars divided on the former but unanimous on the latter.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Allāh is powerful,' do you assume that the power is a real thing in itself, or do you feel unsettled thinking that the relation between Allah and His power might be just a mental construct? How does this affect how you relate to His attributes?
?
Have you ever taken a theological concept for granted—like the fact that Allah knows—without questioning whether that knowledge is a real entity or a mental abstraction? How might your awareness of these philosophical distinctions change the way you internalize what you believe?
?
If the subsistence of power by the essence is merely mental, does that make you feel that your understanding of Allah is less 'real'? Or does it deepen your reliance on the revealed texts rather than your own conceptual frameworks? What will you specifically remember next time you think about Allah's attributes?

Why Clarify Ambiguous Attributes?
The commentator explains that he paraphrases these two attributes because each has multiple meanings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the commentator explains the attributes 'self-subsisting' and 'one' and how their multiple meanings are clarified in Islamic theology.

Core Idea
Ambiguous theological terms require precise clarification to avoid misunderstanding.

In this passage, the commentator explains that the attributes 'qā'im bi nafsih' (self-subsisting) and 'wāḥid' (one) are explained because each has several possible meanings. Without clarification, a student might misunderstand the intended theological meaning. We will see the various meanings and why the commentator chooses to paraphrase them.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the commentator's opening statement about why the author explained these attributes.
The passage is a short explanatory note.
2
Identify the multiple meanings
The passage lists three meanings for 'qā'im' and several unities for 'wāḥid'. List them in your own words.
For 'qā'im': uprightness, consolidation/perfection, severity. For 'wāḥid': personal, specific, generic unity.
3
Understand the clarification
The commentator explains that 'to a locus' means an essence, not a place, and that Allah's independence is derived from His being different from originated things.
This clarifies that 'self-subsisting' means not needing a locus.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(قوله: أي: لَا يَفْتَقِرُ... إلخ) إنما فَسَّرَ المصنف هذه الصفة وما بعدها؛ لأن كلا منهما يُطْلَقُ على معانٍ ؛ إذ الأولى تُطْلَقُ على انتصاب القامة، وعلى: إحكام الشيء وإتقانه، يقال: قام فلان بكذا إذا أَحْكَمَه وأتقنه، وعلى: الشَّدَةِ، يقال: قَامَت الحرب على ساقها إذا اشْتَدَّ أمرُها. والثانيةُ تُطْلَقُ على وحْدَةِ الشخص ووحْدَةِ النوع ووحدة الجنس ونحوها من سائر الوحدات. (وقوله: إِلَى مَحَلَّ أي: ذات يقومُ بها، لا مكانٍ يَحِلُّ فيه؛ لأن عدم افتقاره تعالى إليه مأخوذ من مخالفته تعالى للحوادث.
His saying: 'i.e. does not need... etc.' Indeed, the author explained this attribute and what follows it because each of them is applied to many meanings. For the first (al-qā'im) is applied to the uprightness of stature, and to the consolidation of a thing and its perfection; it is said: 'so-and-so stood (qāma) with such-and-such' if he consolidated it and perfected it; and to severity; it is said: 'the war stood on its leg' if its matter became severe. The second (al-wāḥid) is applied to personal unity, specific unity, generic unity, and similar from among the rest of unities. And his saying: 'to a locus' i.e. an essence in which it subsists, not a place in which it resides; because the lack of His needing it (may He be exalted) is derived from His being different from originated things (al-ḥawādith).
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage is an explanatory note on why the author (the original commentator, likely al-Sanusi or the main commentator) chose to paraphrase the attributes 'qā'im bi nafsih' (self-subsisting) and 'wāḥid' (one). The reason is that each of these terms is ambiguous and can mean several things. For 'qā'im', three meanings are listed: (1) uprightness of stature (physical standing), (2) consolidation and perfection (as in mastering something), (3) severity (as in war becoming intense). The intended meaning in the theological context is 'not needing a locus', as indicated by the phrase 'to a locus' meaning an essence in which it subsists, not a spatial location. The proof that Allah does not need a locus is derived from His being completely different from originated things (al-ḥawādith), which all need a locus. For 'wāḥid', multiple types of unity are mentioned: personal unity (numerical oneness of an individual), specific unity (the members of a species considered as one species), and generic unity (different species under one genus). The author will later specify which kind of unity is intended for Allah.

Key Terms
قيام بنفسه (qā'im bi nafsih)
واحد (wāḥid)
وحدة الشخص (waḥdat al-shakhṣ)
وحدة النوع (waḥdat al-naw')
وحدة الجنس (waḥdat al-jins)
مخالفة للحوادث (mukhālafa lil-ḥawādith)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think 'qā'im bi nafsih' means physically standing or being upright.

Checkpoint
Why did the commentator explain the attribute 'qā'im bi nafsih'?
A) Because it is difficult to understand
B) Because it can mean many things, including uprightness, perfection, and severity
C) Because it is a rare word
D) Because the author made a mistake

Key Takeaway
The commentator clarifies ambiguous terms to ensure the correct theological meaning is understood.

The Four Categories of Existents: Locus and Specifier
Al-Bajuri classifies existents into four categories based on need for locus and specifier.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how theologians classify existents into four types based on their need for a locus and a specifier, and why this matters for understanding Allah's self-subsistence.

Core Idea
Allah alone needs neither a locus nor a specifier; everything else depends on one or both.

Al-Bajuri unpacks a key classification from al-Sanusi: every existent can be placed into one of four categories depending on whether it requires a place (mahall) to inhere in and a cause (mukhaṣṣiṣ) to bring it into existence. This framework helps distinguish the Creator from all created things. First, however, he clarifies two different understandings of the term 'qā'im bi nafsih' (self-subsisting).

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once through, then the English translation. Notice the flow from the two interpretations to the fourfold classification.
The first part about 'qā'im bi nafsih' is a clarification before the main classification.
2
Understand the two interpretations
Identify the two theological definitions of 'self-subsisting': the famous one (not needing a locus or specifier) and another (not needing a locus only, because not needing a specifier follows from pre-eternity).
This shows how precise theological terminology can be.
3
List the four categories
Write down the four divisions: (1) needs neither locus nor specifier (Allah's essence), (2) needs both (accidents of originated things), (3) needs specifier only (substance of originated things), (4) needs locus only (attributes of Allah).
Each category has a clear example given.
4
Reflect on the doctrinal significance
Ask yourself: What does this classification tell us about Allah's uniqueness? How does it help avoid anthropomorphism or denial of attributes?
This connects to the broader theme of tawhid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حاشية البيجوري على السنوسية (وقوله: وَلَا مُخَصّص أي موجود وتفسير قيامه تعالى بنفسه بعدم الافتقار إلى كلّ من المحل والمخصص اصطلاح لبعض المتكلمين، وهو المشهور، وفي اصطلاح بعضهم أنه بمعنى عدم الافتقار إلى المحلّ فقط؛ لأن عدم الافتقار إلى المخصص معلومٌ من صفة القِدَم. واعلم أن الموجودات بالنسبة إلى المحل والمخصص أربعة أقسام كما ذكره المصنف في المقدّمات: قِسْمٌ لا يَفْتَقِرُ إليهما، وهو ذاتُ الله تعالى، وقسم يفتقر إليهما، وهو أعراض الحوادث، وقسم لا يفتقر إلى المَحَلّ ويفتقر إلى المخصص، وهو ذاتُ الحوادث، وقسم يقومُ بالمحلّ ولا يفتقر إلى المخصص وهو صفاتُ الله تعالى
The marginal commentary of al-Bajuri on al-Sanusiyya: And his saying: 'and no specifier (mukhaṣṣiṣ)' i.e. existent. And interpreting His being qā'im bi nafsih as the lack of need for both the locus (maḥall) and the specifier is the terminology of some theologians, and it is the well-known one. In the terminology of some others, it means only the lack of need for the locus, because the lack of need for the specifier is known from the attribute of pre-eternity (qidam). And know that existents, in relation to the locus and the specifier, are four divisions, as the author mentioned in the introductions: a division that does not need either of them, and that is the essence of Allah (may He be exalted); a division that needs both of them, and that is the accidents of originated things (a'rāḍ al-ḥawādith); a division that does not need the locus but needs the specifier, and that is the substance of originated things (dhāt al-ḥawādith); and a division that subsists in the locus and does not need the specifier, and that is the attributes of Allah (may He be exalted).
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage is the main commentary (hashiya) of al-Bajuri on al-Sanusiyya, addressing the attribute 'qā'im bi nafsih' (self-subsisting). It first clarifies the term 'mukhaṣṣiṣ' (specifier) as 'existent'. It then presents two theological interpretations: the famous one (al-mashhūr) that 'self-subsisting' means not needing either a locus (maḥall) or a specifier (mukhaṣṣiṣ); and another opinion that it means not needing a locus only, because not needing a specifier is already known from the attribute of pre-eternity (qidam). Following this, al-Bajuri explains the fourfold classification of existents relative to need for a locus and a specifier, as presented by al-Sanusi in his introductions: (1) That which needs neither – the essence of Allah. (2) That which needs both – the accidents of originated things (a'rāḍ), which inhere in substances and require a specifier to come into existence. (3) That which does not need a locus but needs a specifier – the substance of originated things (jawāhir), i.e., individual bodies. (4) That which subsists in a locus but does not need a specifier – the attributes of Allah (like knowledge, power), which inhere in His essence but are eternal and not caused by an external specifier.

Key Terms
مخصص (mukhaṣṣiṣ)
محل (maḥall)
قيام بنفسه (qā'im bi nafsih)
قدم (qidam)
أعراض (a'rāḍ)
حوادث (ḥawādith)
ذات (dhāt)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think 'self-subsisting' (qā'im bi nafsih) means independent in all respects, without distinguishing between needing a locus and needing a specifier.
Correction: The passage shows a technical distinction: the well-known view is that it means not needing either, but another view says it means only not needing a locus, because not needing a specifier is already known from pre-eternity (qidam). The fourfold classification further clarifies that different existents have different dependencies.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which category of existents needs a specifier but not a locus?
A) The essence of Allah
B) The accidents of originated things
C) The substance of originated things
D) The attributes of Allah

Key Takeaway
Allah alone is absolutely self-sufficient, needing neither a place nor a cause; everything else falls into one of three dependent categories.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a problem, do you instinctively feel self-sufficient, forgetting that you are a contingent being in constant need of Allah's specifier and locus? How can you train your heart to turn to Him first, every time?
?
Allah's attributes like knowledge and power are eternal and need no cause. Do you ever take pride in your own knowledge or power as if they are self-generated? Will you consciously attribute every good quality you have to Allah's gift and sustainance?
?
The category 'substance of originated things' describes you: you exist but need a cause. Does your daily schedule reflect that you live every moment by Allah's will, or do you act as if you are the master of your time and choices? Commit to one concrete change today that acknowledges your complete dependence on Him.

Oneness of Essence: Negating Composition and Multiplicity
Definition of oneness of essence and negation of two types of quantity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meaning of oneness of essence in Islamic theology and how it involves the negation of two types of quantity: continuous and discrete.

Core Idea
God's oneness means no composition and no equal — negating both internal parts and external counterparts.

In this passage, Sanusi begins to unpack the meaning of wahdaniyya (oneness) by focusing on the essence of God. The author explains that oneness of essence means the absence of any composition or multiplicity within God's being. This requires negating two kinds of quantity: continuous quantity (which would imply physical extension) and discrete quantity (which would imply a second being like God). Al-Ajhuri's gloss provides deeper insight into the metaphorical use of 'quantity' here.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Pay attention to the glosses (hashiya) from al-Ajhuri embedded within.
2
Identify the two negated quantities
The passage defines oneness of essence as the negation of two types of quantity: continuous (kamm muttasil) and discrete (kamm munfasil). Find each definition and notice what each implies about God.
Continuous quantity relates to composition; discrete quantity relates to having a counterpart.
3
Understand the metaphorical language
The commentator explains that 'quantity' is used metaphorically: the true cause (composition or multiplicity) is what is negated, and the term 'quantity' is applied to the negation as a figure of speech. Reflect on why this metaphor is necessary.
This clarifies that God is not being described as having or lacking quantity directly.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(قوله: (وَالْوَحْدَانِيَّةُ) أي: في الذات والصفات والأفعال أخذاً من تفسير المصنف - أعني قولَه (أي: لا ثاني له إلخ) ، ويُعْلَمُ من ذلك أن أقسام الوحدانية ثلاثة: وحدانية في الذات، ومعناها: عدم التركب في الذات، وعدم التعدد فيها، فهي عبارة عن نَفْي الكمّ المتّصل في الذات وهو عَرَضُ يقوم بمتصل الأجزاء، وعن نفي الكم المنفصل في الذات وهو عَرَضُ يقوم بمنفصل الأجزاء. حاشية العلامة أحمد الأجهوري (قوله: وَهُوَ عَرَضٌ أي امتداد، وهو يشمل الامتدادات الثلاثة التي هي الطول والعرض والعمق والمراد بالكم المنفي هنا التركب من أجزاء، وإطلاق الكم عليه مجاز من إطلاق اسم المسبب على السبب؛ لأنه لو تركب من أجزاء لقام به الكم الحقيقي الذي هو الامتدادات الثلاثة أو بعضها. (قوله: وَهُوَ عَرَضٌ أي عددٌ بمعنى التعدد والمراد بالكم المنفصل المنفي: النظير، وإطلاق الكم عليه مجاز من إطلاق اسم المسبب على السبب؛ لأنه لو كان هناك نظير الله في الألوهية؛ لقام بهما، أي بالله والنظير عدد أي تعدد، وذلك التعدد هو الكم المنفصل، وبانتفاء النظير انتفى التعدد الذي هو الكم المنفصل. (قوله: بِمُنْفَصِلِ الْأَجْزَاءِ فيه مسامحة؛ لأن الموصوف به أشياء متفرقة لا تركب بينها، فلا تسمى أجزاء.
(His saying: 'And the oneness [wahdaniyya]') i.e., in essence, attributes, and actions, taken from the author's interpretation — I mean his saying (i.e., no second to Him, etc.), and from that it is known that the divisions of oneness are three: oneness in essence, and its meaning is the lack of composition in the essence and the lack of multiplicity in it; so it is an expression of negating the continuous quantity (al-kamm al-muttasil) in the essence, and it is an accident subsisting in connected parts, and of negating the discrete quantity (al-kamm al-munfasil) in the essence, and it is an accident subsisting in separate parts. Gloss of the scholar Ahmad al-Ajhuri: (His saying: 'and it is an accident') i.e., extension, and it includes the three extensions which are length, width, and depth; and what is meant by quantity negated here is composition from parts, and applying 'quantity' to it is a metaphor by way of applying the name of the effect to the cause, because if it were composed of parts, the true quantity which is the three extensions or some of them would subsist in it. (His saying: 'and it is an accident') i.e., number meaning multiplicity; and what is meant by the negated discrete quantity is the counterpart (al-nazir), and applying 'quantity' to it is a metaphor by way of applying the name of the effect to the cause, because if there were a counterpart to God in divinity, then number—i.e., multiplicity—would subsist in both, i.e., in God and the counterpart; and that multiplicity is the discrete quantity, and with the negation of the counterpart, the multiplicity which is the discrete quantity is negated. (His saying: 'in separate parts' [bi-munfasil al-ajza'] is a leniency, because that which is described by it is separate things with no composition between them, so they are not called parts.
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage is a definition and explanation of the first division of oneness (wahdaniyya), specifically oneness of essence. It begins by quoting Sanusi's text that oneness applies to essence, attributes, and actions, and then deduces three types. The focus here is on oneness of essence, meaning the absence of composition and multiplicity in God's essence. This is explained as the negation of two types of quantity: continuous quantity (kamm muttasil), which would imply composition from parts and extension, and discrete quantity (kamm munfasil), which would imply multiplicity or having a counterpart. Al-Ajhuri's gloss clarifies that 'continuous quantity' means extension (length, width, depth) and the true quantity arises from composition; applying the term 'quantity' to the negation is metaphorical because composition is the cause of quantity. Similarly, negating a counterpart negates multiplicity, which is the cause of discrete quantity. The commentator also notes that describing 'separate parts' (munfasil al-ajza') is a leniency because the items are not actually parts, as there is no composition.

Key Terms
وحدانية
كم متصل
كم منفصل
تركب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Because the text mentions 'continuous quantity', some might think God has physical extension or is composed of parts.
Correction: The text explicitly negates continuous quantity, denying any extension or composition. The mention is only to define what is negated.

Checkpoint
What does 'continuous quantity' (al-kamm al-muttasil) refer to in this context?
A) God's power
B) Extension and composition from parts
C) The number of attributes
D) A synonym for 'essence'

Key Takeaway
God's oneness of essence means He is not composed of parts (no continuous quantity) and has no equal (no discrete quantity).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you sometimes mentally split God into 'parts'—His mercy here, His justice there—as if they conflict? How does this lesson on the negation of composition change the way you think about His attributes being one with His essence?
?
Where in your daily life do you unconsciously treat something else as a 'counterpart' to God—seeking ultimate security, validation, or pleasure from a person or thing? What would it take to truly negate that counterpart from your heart?
?
If God has no composition and no equal, then your entire existence depends on a single, indivisible, unparalleled Reality. Are you living as if that is true, or do your actions suggest you believe there are 'parts' to life He doesn't control?

Six Types of Quantum Negated by Oneness
Bajuri explains the deficiency in al-Sanusi's phrase and enumerates six quanta.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that oneness (tawhid) negates six types of quantum: continuous and discrete in essence, attributes, and actions.

Core Idea
Oneness negates every form of multiplicity, whether external or internal.

Bajuri observes that al-Sanusi's phrase 'no second to Him' primarily negates discrete quantum (a separate being), but can be extended to negate continuous quantum (composition within). This leads to the understanding that oneness actually negates six quanta—continuous and discrete in each of essence, attributes, and actions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic text and its literal translation. Notice that Bajuri starts by stating a deficiency in the author's wording.
The phrase 'no second to Him' is immediately understood as negating another separate entity.
2
Understand the deficiency
Bajuri says the immediate understanding (al-mutabadir) only covers discrete quantum. Reflect on why this is a deficiency: it leaves out continuous quantum (internal division).
Discrete quantum means separate individuals; continuous quantum means parts within a single thing.
3
See the possible extension
Bajuri explains that we can extend the meaning to include continuous quantum by saying 'no second to Him neither by connection nor by separation' in essence, attributes, and actions.
This gives a more comprehensive negation of multiplicity.
4
Count the six quanta
Bajuri concludes that the quanta are six: for each of essence, attributes, and actions, there are two types—continuous and discrete. All are negated by oneness.
This is the key takeaway: oneness negates all forms of multiplicity in God.
5
Reflect on the sixth quantum
Bajuri notes that the negation of the sixth (continuous quantum in actions) depends on how it is depicted—if as participation, it is negated; if as multiplicity of acts, it is affirmed (as learned earlier).
This nuance shows careful consideration of divine actions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إذا عَلِمْتَ ذلك؛ عَلِمْتَ أن في قول المصنف (أي: لا ثاني له... إلخ) قصوراً؛ لأن المتبادِرَ منه إنما هو نفي الكمّ المنفصل في الذات والصفات والأفعال، ويُمْكِنُ أن يستفاد منه أيضاً: نفي الكم المتصل في الذات والصفات والأفعال بناءً على تصويره بما ذكر بأن يقال: المراد: لا ثاني له لا اتصالاً ولا انفصالاً في ذاته ولا في صفاته ولا في أفعاله. 2 والحاصل أن الكُمُومَ سِتَّة، وكلُّها منفيَّة بالوحدانية، لكن محله في السادس: : إن صُوّرَ بالمشارَكَة كما علمت فتدبر.
If you know that, you know that in the author's statement (i.e., 'no second to Him... etc.') there is deficiency, because what is immediately understood from it is only the negation of discrete quantum in essence, attributes, and actions. But it is possible to also benefit from it the negation of continuous quantum in essence, attributes, and actions, based on its depiction as mentioned, by saying: the intended meaning is: no second to Him, neither by connection nor by separation, in His essence, nor in His attributes, nor in His actions. And the conclusion is that the quanta are six, and all of them are negated by oneness, but its place in the sixth is if it is depicted as participation, as you have learned, so reflect.
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This passage is an analysis of the author's (al-Sanusi's) phrase 'no second to Him' (la thani lahu) in defining oneness. Bajuri points out a deficiency because the immediate understanding (al-mutabadir) from that phrase is only the negation of discrete quantum (al-kamm al-munfasil) in essence, attributes, and actions. However, it is possible to extend the meaning to also include the negation of continuous quantum (al-kamm al-muttasil) based on how it is depicted. The intended meaning can be: no second to Him, neither by connection (ittisal) nor by separation (infisal), in His essence, attributes, and actions. Then he summarizes that the quanta (kumum) are six: for each of essence, attributes, and actions, there are two types—continuous and discrete. All six are negated by oneness (wahdaniyya). However, concerning the sixth type (which is continuous quantum in actions), its negation depends on how it is depicted: if it is depicted as participation (musharaka) in the same act, it is negated; but if depicted as multiplicity of acts, it is affirmed, as learned earlier.

Key Terms
الكُمُوم
الكم المتصل
الكم المنفصل
الذات
الصفات
الأفعال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The phrase 'no second to Him' only negates another separate being (discrete quantum), but does not address internal composition.
Correction: The phrase can be extended to also negate continuous quantum (internal composition) in essence, attributes, and actions, resulting in six quanta being negated by oneness.

Checkpoint
How many types of quantum are negated by oneness according to the passage?
A) Three
B) Four
C) Six
D) Nine

Key Takeaway
Oneness negates all six forms of quantum—continuous and discrete in essence, attributes, and actions—ensuring that God is free from any multiplicity or composition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If oneness negates every form of multiplicity in God, what does the multiplicity of your own distractions and divided intentions in worship say about the state of your tawheed? Today, will you consciously strive to unify your heart in a single act of devotion?
?
Bajuri teaches that oneness negates continuous quantum in actions only if it is participation. Are there areas in your life where you feel you are 'cooperating' with God rather than submitting to His sole agency? How will you realign your actions to reflect pure tawheed al-af'al?
?
The lesson shows that 'no second to Him' can be misunderstood to exclude only separate beings. Where in your life have you subtly admitted a 'second'—a source of good or power that you rely on alongside God? What concrete step can you take this week to uproot that hidden association?
