Third Answer to Circularity and Synonymity Refutation
Al-Bajuri presents the third answer to circularity and refutes the synonymity objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Bajuri's third answer to circularity distinguishes the act/omission from the object, and how he resolves the objection that ja'iz and mumkin are synonyms.

Core Idea
Al-Bajuri solves circularity by separating the act from its object, and shows that synonymy does not prevent distinct usage.

Al-Bajuri continues to defend Sanusi's definition by offering a third solution to the circularity problem. He carefully distinguishes the act or omission (ja'iz) from the object of that act (mumkin). Then he addresses a separate objection: if ja'iz and mumkin are synonyms, the definition would be redundant. His response is subtle but powerful—both words have multiple meanings, so specific contexts can use them differently without contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it, then read the translation. Let the author's argument unfold naturally.
Notice that the passage contains two distinct moves: a third answer and a refutation of an objection.
2
Identify the third answer
Look for how 'mumkin' in the definition is stripped of the property of possibility, and how 'ja'iz' is equated with the act/omission itself.
The key phrase: 'يراد بالممكن الواقع في التعريف: الشيء بأن يجرد الممكن عن صفة الإمكان'.
3
Understand how circularity is avoided
See that the definer (the act of power) is different from the definition (the object of power). This prevents taking the thing in the definition of itself.
Al-Bajuri says: 'لم يلزم أخذ الشيء في تعريف نفسه المؤدي إلى الدور'.
4
Follow the synonymity objection
The objection is that ja'iz and mumkin are normally synonyms, but the author's definition treats them as different. Grasp the apparent conflict.
The objection is: 'الجائز مرادف للممكن، وكلام المصنف يُفيد أنه مغاير له'.
5
Grasp the resolution
Al-Bajuri explains that both words have two meanings, so they can be synonymous in one sense while being used in distinct specific senses here.
The final sentence: 'كلاً منهما يُطْلَقُ بمعنيين كما علمت'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأجاب بعضهم أيضاً بأن المراد بالممكن الواقع في التعريف: الشيء بأن يجرد الممكن عن صفة الإمكان، فتحصل أن الأجوبة ثلاثة، وأبعدها ما ذكره المحشي تعلق القدرة بالمقدور، وهذا هو المراد بالمعرف، بدليل الإخبار عنه بالفعل، ويطلق ويراد به نفس المقدور - أعني أثر الفعل، وهو المراد بالممكن الواقع في التعريف، وحينئذ لم يلزم أخذ الشيء في تعريف نفسه المؤدي إلى الدور. وبهذا يُجاب عن اعتراض آخَرَ وهو أن الجائز - كما تقرر - مرادف للممكن، وكلام المصنف يُفيد أنه مغاير له؛ لأنه يقتضي أن الجائز نفس الفعل أو الترك، وأن الممكن نفس المفعول أو المتروك، حيث أخبر عن الأول بأنه الفعل أو الترك، وأضاف كلاً منهما إلى الثاني. وتوضيح الجواب: أن إرادة نفس الفعل أو الترك من الجائز، وإرادة نفس المفعول أو المتروك من الممكن؛ لا تُنَافِي أن الجائز مرادف للممكن؛ لأن كلاً منهما يُطْلَقُ بمعنيين كما علمت.
And some also answered that what is meant by the possible (al-mumkin) occurring in the definition is the thing by stripping the possible (al-mumkin) of the property of possibility (al-imkan). So it is obtained that the answers are three, and the farthest of them is what the commentator mentioned: the attachment of power (al-qudra) to the object of power (al-maqdur). And this is what is meant by the definer, evidenced by the informing about it by the act. And it is applied and meant by it the object of power itself—I mean the effect of the act—and what is meant by the possible (al-mumkin) occurring in the definition is that. Then there does not follow taking the thing in the definition of itself leading to circularity (al-dawr). And by this, another objection is answered, which is that the possible (al-ja'iz)—as established—is synonymous with the possible (al-mumkin), but the author's speech indicates that it is different from it; because it requires that the possible (al-ja'iz) is the act itself or the omission, and that the possible (al-mumkin) is the object of the act itself or the object left, where he informed about the first that it is the act or omission, and added each of them to the second. And the clarification of the answer is that intending by al-ja'iz the act itself or the omission, and intending by al-mumkin the object of the act itself or the object left, does not contradict that al-ja'iz is synonymous with al-mumkin; because each of them is applied in two meanings, as you have learned.
Source pages p. 142

Commentary
This passage presents the third answer to the circularity objection and then addresses a separate objection about synonymity. The third answer proposes that the term 'mumkin' in the definition is used in a stripped sense: it refers to the thing after removing the property of possibility (imkan). The commentator then elaborates by mentioning 'the attachment of power to the object of power' (ta'alluq al-qudra bi al-maqdur), which is the intended definer (al-mu'arraf). This is supported by the fact that the definer is described as an act: the act or omission is itself the ja'iz, while the object of that act (the maqdur) is the mumkin. By distinguishing the definer (the act) from the definition (the object), circularity is avoided. Then a second objection is raised: ja'iz and mumkin are usually considered synonyms, but the author's phrasing makes them appear different (ja'iz = act/omission, mumkin = object). The answer is that both words have two meanings. Ja'iz can mean the act/omission (in this context) or the general category of what is possible; similarly, mumkin can mean the object or the general category. The two meanings do not conflict; thus, ja'iz and mumkin can still be synonymous in one sense while being used here in distinct specific senses.

Key Terms
تجريد (tajrid)
تعلق (ta'alluq)
قدرة (qudra)
مقدور (maqdur)
مرادف (muradif)
إطلاق (itlaq)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if ja'iz and mumkin are synonyms, then the distinction made by the author is contradictory or that the third answer fails.
Correction: The passage explains that both words have multiple meanings, so they can be synonymous in a general sense while being used in different specific senses here. The specific usage (ja'iz for act, mumkin for object) does not negate the general synonymy.

Checkpoint
How does the third answer avoid circularity?
A) By redefining ja'iz as the act/omission and mumkin as the object, thus making the definer and definition different.
B) By stating that ja'iz and mumkin are completely synonymous.
C) By rejecting the definition entirely.
D) By adding more conditions to the definition.

Key Takeaway
Al-Bajuri defends Sanusi's definition by showing that the act/omission (ja'iz) and its object (mumkin) are distinct, and that ja'iz and mumkin can be synonymous yet used in specific senses without contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Distinguishing al-Ja'iz and al-Mumkin in Sanusi's Definition
Al-Bajuri clarifies the distinction between al-ja'iz and al-mumkin in Sanusi's definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Bajuri resolves the apparent conflict between the synonymy of al-ja'iz and al-mumkin and their distinct usage in Sanusi's definition.

Core Idea
One term, two meanings: synonymy and distinction coexist.

Al-Bajuri tackles a subtle objection: if al-ja'iz and al-mumkin are synonyms, why does Sanusi define them differently? In this passage, he explains that each term carries two senses—one relational, one entitative—allowing them to be both synonymous and distinct. This careful linguistic analysis prevents a misunderstanding that could undermine the entire definitional framework.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow, then read the English translation. Notice how al-Bajuri first addresses circularity before moving to the synonymy objection.
The passage has two main parts: a clarification of 'definer' and a response to the synonymy objection.
2
Identify the objection
Find the sentence where al-Bajuri states the objection: that al-ja'iz is synonymous with al-mumkin, yet Sanusi's wording seems to treat them as different. Underline this objection.
The objection hinges on the apparent difference in definition.
3
Understand the answer's logic
Al-Bajuri's answer is that each term has two meanings. al-ja'iz can refer to the act/omission itself, while al-mumkin can refer to the object of the act/omission. In one sense they overlap, in another they are distinct. Trace this reasoning.
This preserves synonymy without contradicting the definition.
4
Reflect on the key insight
Consider why this distinction matters for kalam. Without it, the definition would be circular or self-contradictory. al-Bajuri shows that linguistic precision is essential in theological concepts.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تعلق القدرة بالمقدور ، وهذا هو المراد بالمعرف، بدليل الإخبار عنه بالفعل، ويُطلق ویراد به نفس المقدور - أعني أثر الفعل ، وهو المراد بالممكن الواقع في التعريف، وحينئذ لم يلزمُ أَخُذُ الشيء في تعريف نفسه المؤدي إلى الدور.
وبهذا يُجاب عن اعتراض آخَرَ وهو أن الجائز - كما تقرر - مرادف للممكن، وكلامُ المصنف يُفيدُ أنه مغاير له؛ لأنه يقتضي أن الجائز نفسُ الفعل أو الترك، وأن الممكن نفس المفعول أو المتروك، حيث أخبر عن الأول بأنه الفعل أو الترك، وأضاف كلاً منهما إلى الثاني.
وتوضيح الجواب: أن إرادة نفس الفعل أو الترك من الجائز، وإرادة نفس المفعول أو المتروك من الممكن؛ لا تُنَافِي أن الجائز مرادف للممكن؛ لأن كلاً منهما يُطْلَقُ بمعنيين كما علمت.
The relation of power (al-qudra) to the object of power (al-maqdur) – this is what is meant by the definer (al-muʿarrif), as evidenced by the predicate [being] an act. And it [the definer] is also used to mean the object of power itself – i.e., the effect of the act – and that is what is meant by the possible (al-mumkin) occurring in the definition. Then there is no necessity of taking a thing in its own definition, leading to circularity. And by this, another objection is answered, namely that the permissible (al-jaʾiz) – as established – is synonymous with the possible, and the author's statement indicates that it is different from it; because it requires that the permissible is the act itself or the omission, and that the possible is the object of the act or the omitted thing, since he [the author] reported the first as the act or omission, and added each of them to the second. The clarification of the answer: that intending the act itself or the omission from the permissible, and intending the object of the act or the omitted thing from the possible, does not contradict that the permissible is synonymous with the possible; because each of them is used in two meanings, as you have learned.
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage is a definition and refutation. It begins by explaining that the term 'definer' (al-muʿarrif) in Sanusi's original definition refers to the relation of power (taʿalluq al-qudra) to the object of power (al-maqdur). It then clarifies that 'al-mumkin' (the possible) in the definition refers to the object of power itself, i.e., the effect of the act. This avoids circularity. Next, al-Bajuri addresses an objection: if al-jaʾiz (the permissible) is synonymous with al-mumkin, why does Sanusi's definition treat them differently? The answer is that each term has two meanings: al-jaʾiz can mean the act or omission itself (relational), and al-mumkin can mean the object of the act or omission (entity). In one sense they are synonymous, in another they are distinct. This resolution preserves both the synonymy and the differentiation.

Correction: Al-Bajuri shows they share a core meaning (both refer to what is possible) but differ in aspect: one relates to the act itself, the other to the object of the act.

Objection and answer on the proof structure
Sanusi refutes an objection about the proof's logical form.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi defends the logical structure of the proof for God's existence against an objection, showing that both a simple and a compound formulation are valid.

Core Idea
The proof works either simply or compoundly because createdness is the aspect of indication.

This passage presents a logical objection that might confuse beginners: Is the proof for God's existence a single term or a compound argument? The objector claims both forms fail. Sanusi's answer clarifies that the proof is valid in both forms, provided we understand that 'createdness' (ḥudūth) is the aspect (jiha) that indicates the Creator. This response not only defends the proof but teaches a deeper lesson about how theological arguments are structured.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and its translation without analysis. Let the objection and answer flow together.
Notice the objector presents two alternatives: single term (mufrad) or compound (murakkab).
2
Map the objection
Identify the two horns of the objection: if the proof is a single term, it is the world itself (not its createdness); if compound, it is two premises. In either case the objector says it's wrong.
The objector is trying to show a flaw in the proof's formulation.
3
Follow the answer's two tracks
See how Sanusi answers each case: First, the proof can be taken as a simple expression where 'the world from its createdness' serves as proof because createdness is the aspect of indication. Second, it can be taken as compound with the minor premise stated and the major premise implied but proved later.
Key term: 'جهة الدلالة' (aspect of indication) is central to the first response.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَحُدُوثُ الْعَالَمِ اعتُرضَ: بأنه إن جُعِلَ الدليل مفرداً؛ فهو العالم لا حدوثه، وإن جعل مركباً؛ فهو المركَّب من مقدمتين قائلتين: العالم حادث، وكلُّ حادث لا بُدَّ له من مُحْدِثٍ، وعلى كلّ ; فكلامُهُ غير صحيح. وأجيب بأنه يمكن إجراؤه على الأول، لكن لما كان الحدوث هو جهةً الدلالة؛ كان كأنه هو الدليل، وعلى هذا فالمعنى: فالعالَمُ من حيث حدوثه، وحينئذ ففي كلامه إشارة إلى أن جهة دلالة العالم على وجوده تعالى: هي حدوثه لا إمكانه مثلاً. ويمكن إجراؤُهُ على الثاني؛ لأن حدوث العالم في قوة الصغرى القائلة: العالم حادث، ولا بُدَّ من أن يَنْضَمَّ إليها الكبرى القائلة وكل حادث لا بُدَّ له من مُحْدِثٍ، فيكون قد أشار إلى الصغرى وحَذَفَ الكبرى، لكنه ذَكَرَ دليلها بقوله: (لأنه لو لم یکن له محدث... إلخ).
So the world's createdness was objected to: that if the proof is made a single term, it is the world not its createdness; and if made compound, it is the compound of two premises saying: the world is created, and every created thing necessarily has a creator; and in any case, his speech is not correct. And it was answered that it can be applied to the first, but since createdness is the aspect of indication, it is as if it is the proof; and on this basis the meaning is: the world from the aspect of its createdness; and then in his speech there is an indication that the aspect of the world's indication of His existence (may He be exalted) is its createdness, not its possibility, for example. And it can be applied to the second, because the world's createdness has the force of the minor premise saying: the world is created, and it must be joined by the major premise saying: and every created thing necessarily has a creator; so he indicated the minor premise and omitted the major premise, but he mentioned its proof by his saying: (because if it had no creator... etc.).
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer regarding the structure of the proof for God's existence based on the world's createdness. The objector argues that if the proof is taken as a single term (mufrad), then it is the world itself, not its createdness; if taken as compound (murakkab), it consists of two premises. In either case, the objector claims the formulation is incorrect. The answer explains that the proof can be understood in two ways. First, it can be taken as a simple expression where 'the world from the aspect of its createdness' functions as the proof, because createdness is the aspect (jiha) that indicates the Creator. Second, it can be taken as a compound proof where the minor premise ('the world is created') is stated and the major premise ('every created thing needs a creator') is implied, but its proof is given in the following statement ('because if it had no creator...'). Thus, the proof is valid either way.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof must be either purely simple or purely compound; one of the interpretations must be incorrect.
Correction: Both interpretations are valid because the aspect of createdness allows the proof to be expressed simply, and the compound form is valid by implication of the major premise.

The proof for God's existence from the world's createdness can be expressed simply (with createdness as aspect) or as a compound argument; both are logically sound.

Three Opinions on the Aspect of Indication
Al-Anbabii lists three opinions on what aspect of the world proves God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three opinions on which aspect of the world serves as evidence for God's existence and why only specific aspects are relevant.

Core Idea
Not every aspect of the world points to God—only its createdness and possibility.

Al-Anbabii clarifies a crucial point in the proof for God's existence from the world: the proof is not the world itself but a specific aspect of it. He lists three opinions on which aspect—createdness, possibility, or both—carries the evidential weight. This distinction prevents confusion and refines our understanding of how the world indicates its Creator.

Guided Path
1
Understand 'aspect of indication'
Learn what jiha al-dalala means and why it matters for the proof of God's existence.
Aspect of indication is the specific property that makes something a valid proof.
2
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how al-Anbabii distinguishes between aspects that lead to the conclusion and those that don't.
Pay attention to the list of non-leading aspects like length and density.
3
Identify the three opinions
From the passage, find the three opinions on which aspect of the world proves God: createdness alone, possibility alone, or the combination of both.
The passage says 'on the difference of opinion' indicating scholarly disagreement.
4
Reflect on the grammatical point
Note the remark about 'the world from the aspect of its createdness' being an annexation of the attribute to the described. This emphasizes that it is the quality, not the substance, that proves God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقوال ثلاثة في جهة الدلالة.
تقريرات العلامة محمد الأنبايي
(قوله: فَهُوَ الْعَالَمُ لَا حُدُوثُهُ) وذلك لأن الدليل هو ما احتوى على الموصل للمطلوب، فالعالَمُ مثلاً دليل على وجوده تعالى؛ لاحتوائه على جهات، منها ما لا يوصِلُ للمقصود كطُولِهِ وكثافته وبساطته وتركيبه وبياضه وسواده ومنها ما يوصل كحدوثه، أو إمكانه أو مجموع الحدوث والإمكان على الخلاف. (قوله: فَالْمَعْنَى : فَالْعَالَمُ مِنْ حَيْثُ حُدُوثِهِ أقرب منه: أنه من إضافة الصفة إلى الموصوف.
Three sayings concerning the aspect of indication.
Annotations of the scholar Muhammad al-Anbabii
(His saying: so it is the world, not its createdness) And that is because the proof is that which contains the means to the desired conclusion; so the world, for example, is a proof for His existence (may He be exalted) because it contains aspects, among them what does not lead to the intended conclusion, like its length, density, simplicity, composition, whiteness, and blackness; and among them what does lead, like its createdness, or its possibility, or the combination of createdness and possibility, according to difference of opinion. (His saying: so the meaning: the world from the aspect of its createdness is nearer [to being] from adding the attribute to the described.
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage classifies three opinions regarding the aspect (jiha) of the world that serves as evidence for God's existence. Al-Anbabii explains that a proof must contain the means to reach the desired conclusion. The world has many aspects: some do not lead to God (like length, density, simplicity, composition, color), while others do lead, namely its createdness, its possibility, or both combined. The reference is to a previous remark that 'the world from the aspect of its createdness' is more appropriately understood as an annexation of the attribute (createdness) to the described (the world), emphasizing that the proof is not the world per se but its qualified aspect.

Key Terms
جهة الدلالة
الحدوث
الإمكان
الدليل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any attribute of the world (e.g., its length, color) can be used as evidence for God's existence.
Correction: Only specific aspects—createdness, possibility, or both—serve as valid proofs; other attributes like length or density do not lead to the intended conclusion.

The Structure of the Creation Argument in Kalam
Al-Bajuri explains the number of proofs for each premise of the creation argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the proof for the world's creation is built on a minor premise requiring two sub-proofs (bodies and accidents) and a major premise requiring one proof.

Core Idea
The creation argument stands on two pillars: showing the world is created and that every created thing needs a Creator.

Al-Bajuri explains that the proof for the world's creation is not a single step but has a clear logical structure. The minor premise ('the world is created') itself rests on two evidences: the createdness of bodies and the createdness of accidents. The major premise ('every created thing needs a creator') needs only one proof. This careful breakdown shows the precision of kalam argumentation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the minor and major premises
Read the Arabic passage and translation to see how the author distinguishes between the two premises and the number of proofs needed.
Notice the contrast: the major premise is simple, the minor premise is compound.
2
Identify the two sub-proofs of the minor premise
The minor premise has two claims: the createdness of bodies (ajsam) and the createdness of accidents (a'rad). Each has a separate proof cited by the author.
This shows the world is composed of bodies and accidents, both proven to be non-eternal.
3
Understand the major premise proof
The major premise is supported by the statement 'because if it had no creator...' which is a concise proof that anything that comes into existence requires an efficient cause.
This is a foundational principle in kalam.
4
Reflect on the overall argument structure
The full argument is: the world is created (minor), and every created thing has a creator (major), therefore the world has a creator.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد استدل على الصغرى أيضاً بقوله : ودليل حدوث العالم... إلخ)، وقَدَّمَ دليل الكبرى لقلة الكلام عليه فإنها لا تحتاج إلا إلى دليل واحد، وأما الصُّغْرَى؛ فتحتاج إلى دليلين؛ لأنها في قُوَّةِ دَعْوَتَين: الأولى: حدوثُ الأجرام، وقد استدل عليها بقوله: (ودليل حدوث العالم... إلخ)، والثانية: حدوث الأعراض، وقد استدل عليها بقوله: ( ودليل حدوث الأعراض... إلخ).
(قوله: لأَنَّهُ لَوْ لَمْ يَكُنْ لَهُ مُحْدِثٌ إلخ) قد عرفت أن هذا دليل الكبرى القائلة: وكلُّ حادثٍ لا بُدَّ له من مُحْدِثِ.
(قوله: بَلْ حَدَثَ بِنَفْسِهِ) هذا أخصُّ مما قبله؛ لأن نَفْي المُحْدِث للعالم يصدق بحدوثه بنفسه وبقِدَمِه، لكن لما كان إبطال الثاني مأخوذاً من قوله: (ودليل حدوث العالم إلخ)، والمقصود بالدليل المذكور إنما هو إبطال الأول؛ خَصَّهُ بالإضراب.
حاشية العلامة أحمد الأجهوري
(قوله: وَبِقِدَمِهِ... إلخ) صدقه بالقدم مع أن الفرض أنه حادث كما دلت عليه الصغرى لا يظهر، فالضمير في قوله: (لأنه عائد على العالم الموصوف بالحدوث، فكان الأولى - كما في الدسوقي على المصنف" - أن يجعل وجه العموم صدقه بحدوثه بنفسه وبإحداثه نفسه، فلذلك أضرب عنه بقوله : (بل حدث بنفسه، وترك الشق الثاني؛ لأنه بديهي البطلان.
تقريرات العلامة محمد الأنبابي.
:(قوله : لأَنَّ نَفْي الْمُحْدِثِ لِلْعَالَم يَصْدُقُ بِحُدُوثِهِ بِنَفْسِهِ وَبِقِدَمِهِ) فيه: أن الضمير عائد على كل حادث؛ إذ المقصود من قوله لأنه لو لم يكن له محدث... إلخ): أنه لو انتفى محمول الكبرى عن موضعها الذي هو الحادث؛ لَزِمَ أن يكون... إلخ، فحينئذ لا يقال: إنه يَصْدُقُ بالقدم، فالأولى أن يقول: لأن نَفْيَ مُحْدِثِ الحادث صادق بما إذا أحدث نفسه، وبما إذا كان حدوثه لنفسه بأن كان اتفاقياً ولم يؤثر فيه شيء لا نفسه ولا غيرها، فتكون (بل) للانتقال من الأعمّ إلى الأخص ، وإنما انتقل للثاني دون الأول؛ لأنه ضروري الاستحالة
هذا إذا جَرَيْنا على المتبادر من قوله (وكل حادث لا بد له من (محدث)، فإنه يتبادَرُ
And he also provided evidence for the minor premise by his saying: 'And the proof of the world's createdness... etc.' And he presented the proof of the major premise first because discussion of it is brief, for it needs only one proof; as for the minor premise, it needs two proofs, because it has the force of two claims: the first: the createdness of bodies, and he provided evidence for it by his saying: (And the proof of the world's createdness... etc.); and the second: the createdness of accidents, and he provided evidence for it by his saying: (And the proof of the createdness of accidents... etc.)
(His saying: for if it had no creator... etc.) You have known that this is the proof of the major premise stating: and every created thing necessarily has a creator.
(His saying: rather it occurred by itself) This is more specific than what preceded it, because the denial of a creator for the world is true of its occurring by itself and of its eternity; but since the refutation of the second is taken from his saying: (And the proof of the world's createdness... etc.), and the intended meaning of the mentioned proof is only the refutation of the first; he specified it by the shift (idrab).
Gloss of the scholar Ahmad al-Ajhuri
(His saying: and its eternity... etc.) Its being true of eternity, despite the assumption that it is created as the minor premise indicated, is not apparent. So the pronoun in his saying: (because it) refers to the world described by createdness; so it would be better - as in al-Dusuqi on the author - to make the basis of generality its being true of its occurring by itself and of its bringing itself into existence; therefore he shifted from it by his saying: (rather it occurred by itself), and left the second case because it is obviously invalid.
Annotations of the scholar Muhammad al-Anbabii
(His saying: for the denial of a creator for the world is true of its occurring by itself and of its eternity) In it: that the pronoun refers to every created thing; since the meaning of his saying 'because if it had no creator... etc.' is that if the predicate of the major premise were removed from its subject which is the created thing, it would necessarily be... etc.; then it cannot be said that it is true of eternity. So it is better to say: because the denial of a creator for the created thing is true in the case when it brings itself into existence, and in the case when its occurrence is by itself, as it happened by chance and nothing affected it, neither itself nor another. So 'bal' (rather) is for transition from the more general to the more specific; and he only moved to the second without the first because it is necessarily impossible.
This is if we proceed according to the immediate understanding of his saying (and every created thing necessarily has a creator), for it is understood
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage details the structure of the proof for the world's createdness. It explains that the minor premise ('the world is created') is established by two sub-proofs: one for the createdness of bodies (ajsam) and one for the createdness of accidents (a'rad). The major premise ('every created thing needs a creator') requires only one proof, which is given as 'because if it had no creator...' The text then discusses the phrase 'rather it (the world) occurred by itself' (bal hadatha bi-nafsihi). This is more specific than the previous denial of a creator, because denying a creator could mean either the world is self-created or eternal. But since the proof for the world's createdness already refutes eternity, the focus is on refuting self-creation. The glosses by al-Ajhuri and al-Anbabii refine this logic: the pronoun in the argument is best understood as referring to 'every created thing', not the world specifically, and the shift (idrab) from 'if it had no creator' to 'rather it occurred by itself' is a transition from the general impossibility (no creator at all) to the specific impossibility (self-creation). The discussion ensures precision in the argumentation.

Correction: The author clarifies it requires two proofs because it covers both bodies and accidents.

The Logical Nuance of 'Bal Hadatha bi-Nafsihi' in Kalam
Al-Bajuri and his commentators refine the logical implications of the major premise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author uses the phrase 'rather, it occurred by itself' and how it refines the argument against the world being creatorless.

Core Idea
The phrase 'rather, it occurred by itself' is a precise shift that eliminates a specific impossibility.

In proving that the world needs a creator, the argument denies that it lacks a creator. But what exactly is being denied? Al-Bajuri explains that denying a creator could mean the world is either eternal or self-created. Since the world's creation has already been established, the denial targets the self-creation possibility. The glossators further refine this logic, showing the pronoun refers to 'every created thing' and the shift is from a general impossibility to a more specific one.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage starting from 'bal hadatha bi-nafsihi'
Read this part of the text along with the translation to understand the point about the shift (idrab).
The phrase 'bal' indicates a shift from a broader denial to a more specific one.
2
Identify the two possibilities denied by the earlier phrase
The earlier 'if it had no creator' could imply the world is eternal or self-created. The author specifies that only self-creation is intended here.
Eternity is already refuted by the proof of the world's createdness.
3
Examine the gloss by al-Ajhuri
He questions the grammar and suggests the pronoun should refer to the world as described by createdness, and the shift is to self-origination.
4
Examine the gloss by al-Anbabii
He argues the pronoun refers to 'every created thing' and the denial of a creator is true in two cases: self-creation or random occurrence. The shift moves from the more general (no creator) to the more specific (self-creation).
This clarifies the logical precision of the argument.
5
Reflect on the importance of this nuance
This shows that kalam arguments are carefully worded to avoid any logical loopholes.

Every Temporal Being Needs a Creator
Commentary on the proof of the major premise of the cosmological argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi proves that every temporal being must have a creator, and why the phrase 'rather, it came to be by itself' is a precise formulation.

Core Idea
Every event requires a cause; denying a creator doesn't automatically mean self-causation.

In this passage, the commentator unpacks Sanusi's proof of the major premise of the cosmological argument. He clarifies the logical relationship between rejecting a creator and the two alternatives: self-causation or eternity. This lesson will help you see the precise reasoning behind the statement that every temporal being needs a creator.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage carefully, then its translation. Notice that the passage has two main parts: the statement of the major premise and the comment on the phrase 'bal hadatha bi nafsihi'.
The Arabic uses the particle 'bal' (بل) which signals a shift to a more specific case.
2
Identify the major premise
The passage states: 'This is the proof of the major premise which states: Every temporal being necessarily has a creator.' Recognize that this is the claim being defended.
3
Understand the specificity
The commentator explains why 'rather, it came to be by itself' is more specific than simply 'if it had no creator'. Denying a creator could imply either self-causation or eternity, but eternity is already refuted by another proof, so the focus narrows to self-causation.
The Arabic word 'إضراب' (turning away) indicates this shift in focus.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(قوله: لأَنَّهُ لَوْ لَمْ يَكُنْ لَهُ مُحْدِثٌ إلخ) قد عرفت أن هذا دليل الكبرى القائلة: وكلُّ حادثٍ لا بُدَّ له من مُحْدِثِ. (قوله: بَلْ حَدَثَ بِنَفْسِهِ) هذا أخصُّ مما قبله؛ لأن نَفْي المُحْدِث للعالم يصدق بحدوثه بنفسه وبقدَمه، لكن لما كان إبطال الثاني مأخوذاً من قوله: (ودليل حدوث العالم إلخ)، والمقصود بالدليل المذكور إنما هو إبطال الأول؛ خَصَّهُ بالإضراب.
(His saying: 'For if it had no creator...') You have known that this is the proof of the major premise which states: Every temporal being necessarily has a creator. (His saying: 'Rather, it came to be by itself') This is more specific than what precedes it, because the negation of a creator for the world applies to its coming to be by itself and to its eternity. But since the refutation of the second [eternity] is taken from his saying: 'And the proof of the temporality of the world...', and the intended purpose of the mentioned proof is only to refute the first [self-causation], he specified it by turning away.

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on Sanusi's proof of the major premise of the cosmological argument: every temporal being requires a creator. The commentator explains the structure: the major premise is that every event must have a cause. Sanusi's statement 'rather, it came to be by itself' is more specific than simply denying a creator, because denying a creator could mean either the world is self-caused or eternal. However, since the proof of the world's temporality already eliminates eternity, the focus here is on refuting self-causation. The Arabic particle 'bal' (rather) indicates a shift to a more specific case.

Key Takeaway

Impossibility of Preference Without a Preponderant
Sanusi refutes self-causation using the principle of equal alternatives.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the principle of equal alternatives makes self-causation impossible for possible beings.

Core Idea
A possible being cannot cause itself because existence and non-existence are equal—preference requires a cause.

Sanusi's argument against self-causation rests on a powerful rational principle: preference without a preponderant is impossible. This lesson unpacks that principle through the lens of equal alternatives. The commentary clarifies the key analogy of a balanced scale, showing why self-causation leads to logical contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Understand the equal alternatives principle
Read the passage and note that existence and non-existence are considered equal for a possible being. This is the foundational premise of the argument.
The commentator mentions this is the well-known view, with an alternative view discussed later.
2
Apply the principle to self-causation
If the world had no creator and came to be by itself, it would mean one of two equal alternatives (existence) was preferred over the other (non-existence) without a cause.
This leads to the impossibility of preference without a preponderant.
3
Grasp the analogy of the balanced scale
The commentator compares this to a scale with two equal pans: if one pan outweighs the other without any added weight, that is impossible. Similarly, preference without cause is impossible.
This analogy helps visualize the logical contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(قوله: لَزِمَ أَنْ يَكُونَ أَحَدُ الْأَمْرَيْنِ الْمُتَسَاوِيَيْنِ) أي: اللذين هما: الوجود والعدمُ، والمراد بأحدهما الوجود، والمراد بصاحبه العَدَمُ، وهذا ـ كما ترى - مبني على أن الوجود والعدم بالنظر إلى ذات الممكن سيان، وهو المشهور. وقيل : العَدَمُ راجح لأسبقيته، واللازم على هذا القول: لو لم يكن للعالم مُحْدِثٌ، بل حَدَثَ بنفسه؛ ترجّحَ المرجوحُ بلا سبب وهو أقوى في الاستحالة من اللازم على القول الأول. (قوله: وَهُوَ مُحَالٌ) أي: لما فيه من اجتماع الرُّجْحَان والمساواة، وهما ضدان، ونظيرُ ذلك: ميزان اعتدَلَتْ كفتاه ورَجَحَتْ إحداهما على الأخرى بلا سبب.
(His saying: 'It would necessitate one of the two equal alternatives') Meaning: the two which are existence and non-existence. What is intended by 'one of them' is existence, and by 'its counterpart' is non-existence. This—as you see—is based on the view that existence and non-existence with respect to the essence of a possible being are equal, and this is the well-known view. And it is said: non-existence is preponderant due to its priority. The necessary consequence on this view: if the world had no creator, but rather came to be by itself, then the preponderated [existence] would be preponderated without cause, and that is stronger in impossibility than the necessary consequence on the first view. (His saying: 'And it is impossible') Meaning: because of the combination of preponderance and equality in it, and they are opposites. The analogy for that is a scale whose two pans are balanced and one of them outweighs the other without cause.
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on Sanusi's argument that if the world were self-caused, it would involve impossible preference without a preponderant. The commentator clarifies Sanusi's phrase 'one of the two equal alternatives' as existence and non-existence. The well-known position is that for a possible being, existence and non-existence are equal in themselves. On this view, if the world came to be by itself, existence would be preferred over non-existence without any cause, which is impossible—like a balanced scale tipping without reason. The commentator also mentions an alternative position that non-existence has priority, making self-causation even more impossible because it would prefer the less likely option without cause. The impossibility arises from the contradiction of combining equality and preference.

Key Terms
ممكن
وجود
عدم
ترجيح بلا مرجح
ميزان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The principle of equal alternatives applies to all beings, including necessary beings like God.
Correction: The principle only applies to possible beings (mumkin), whose existence and non-existence are equal in themselves. Necessary beings (wajib) have a different rational status.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, what is the analogy given for the impossibility of preference without a cause?
A) A scale with two equal pans, one of which tips without a weight.
B) A rolling ball that stops without friction.
C) A fire that burns without oxygen.
D) A human who acts without a motive.

Key Takeaway
The principle of equal alternatives shows that a possible being cannot be self-caused, as that would require preference without a cause, which is rationally impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your own existence is not self-caused but depends entirely on an external Cause, what does that demand from you in how you live every moment? Are you acknowledging that dependence, or do you act as if you are self-sufficient?
?
Consider the scale of your own life: every breath, every heartbeat is a tipping of the balance from non-existence to existence. What cause keeps that scale tipped? How does the certainty of that Cause change your response to hardship or ease today?
?
When you look at your own desires and choices, are you ever guilty of acting as if your preferences come from nowhere—as if you can choose without a reason? How does the principle of 'preference without a preponderant' expose the need to align your will with the Truth?

The Syllogistic Proof for the Temporality of Bodies
Sanusi explains the syllogistic proof for the temporality of bodies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi proves that physical bodies are temporal through their necessary connection to temporal accidents using a syllogistic argument.

Core Idea
Bodies are temporal because they are inseparable from temporal accidents.

Sanusi presents a concise syllogistic proof for the temporality of the physical world. He focuses specifically on bodies (ajsam), arguing that since bodies always accompany accidents that are known to be temporal, the bodies themselves must be temporal. This lesson unpacks the premises and conclusion of this argument, showing how the proof works.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice that the commentator is explaining Sanusi's original proof by breaking it into premises.
Pay attention to the terms 'ملازمة' (connection) and 'حادث' (temporal).
2
Identify the two premises
Find the minor premise: bodies are connected to temporal accidents. Find the major premise: everything connected to a temporal thing is temporal.
The commentator marks these as 'صغرى' and 'كبرى'.
3
Understand the conclusion
See how the two premises combine to yield the conclusion: bodies are temporal. This is a direct application of syllogistic logic.
The result is 'الأجرام حادثة' (bodies are temporal).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(قوله: وَدَلِيلُ حُدُوثِ الْعَالَمِ... إلخ) قد عرفت أن هذا دليل على حدوث الأجرام، فالمراد من العالم هنا خصوص الأجرام، بخلافه فيما تقدم، فإن المراد به: ما يَشْمَلُ الأجرام والأعراض. (قوله: مُلَازَمَتُهُ لِلْأَعْرَاضِ الْحَادِثَةِ في قوَّة الصغرى القائلة: الأجرامُ ملازِمَةٌ للأعراض الحادثة. (وقوله: وَمُلَازِمُ الْحَادِثِ حَادِثٌ في قوَّة الكبرى القائلة: وكلُّ ما لازَمَ الحادث حادث، فيصير نَظْمُ الدليل هكذا الأجرام ملازمةٌ للأعراض الحادثة، وكلُّ ما لازم الحادث حادث، ونتيجته: الأجرام حادثة.
(His saying: 'And the proof of the temporality of the world...') You have known that this is a proof for the temporality of bodies. So by 'world' here is meant specifically bodies, unlike in what preceded, where it meant what includes bodies and accidents. (His saying: 'Its connection to temporal accidents' is in the force of the minor premise saying: Bodies are connected to temporal accidents. (And his saying: 'And whatever is connected to a temporal thing is temporal' is in the force of the major premise saying: Everything connected to a temporal thing is temporal. Thus the arrangement of the proof becomes: Bodies are connected to temporal accidents, and everything connected to a temporal thing is temporal, and its result: Bodies are temporal.
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage is a commentary explaining Sanusi's proof for the temporality of the physical world. The proof is based on the idea that bodies (ajsam) are inseparable from accidents (a'rad) which are known to be temporal. The commentator clarifies that 'world' here refers specifically to bodies, not the broader sense including accidents. The proof has a syllogistic structure: the minor premise is that bodies are always connected to temporal accidents; the major premise is that anything connected to a temporal thing is itself temporal; the conclusion is that bodies are temporal. The commentator explicitly maps Sanusi's phrases to the premises and conclusion of the syllogism.

Misconception: The proof requires that bodies themselves have accidents at every moment.
Correction: The proof only requires that bodies are always in a state of being connected to some accident that is temporal, not that they have accidents at every moment.

What is the minor premise in the proof for the temporality of bodies?
A) Bodies are connected to temporal accidents.
B) Everything connected to a temporal thing is temporal.
C) Accidents are temporal.
D) The world includes both bodies and accidents.

Key Takeaway
The temporality of bodies is proven by their constant connection to temporal accidents through a simple syllogism.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The proof shows that everything around you is temporal. Yet you act as if your furniture, your health, and your relationships will last forever. What is one possession or comfort you cling to that you could actively release today—either physically or emotionally—as a step toward living the truth of this lesson?
?
If the syllogism is sound, then your existence is contingent and fleeting. Does that thought terrify you or liberate you? What would it take for you to welcome the temporality of your life as a call to prepare for what is eternal, rather than to despair?

The Two Equal Possibilities: Existence and Non-Existence
Al-Bajuri introduces the two equal possibilities and the well-known view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the equality of existence and non-existence for a contingent being leads to the necessity of a cause for the world.

Core Idea
If existence and non-existence are equal, only a cause can tip the balance.

This lesson unpacks the foundational argument that if the world had no creator, one of two equal possibilities would have to be chosen without reason. Al-Bajuri explains that existence and non-existence are considered equal with respect to the essence of a contingent being, setting the stage for the impossibility of preference without a cause.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the two equal possibilities
Read the Arabic passage once and its translation. Notice that Al-Bajuri identifies the two equal possibilities as existence and non-existence.
The term 'equal possibilities' refers to the state of a contingent being before a cause determines it.
2
Understand the well-known view
The passage states that the well-known view is that existence and non-existence are equal with respect to the essence of the contingent. This is crucial for the argument.
3
Grasp the conclusion
If the world had no originator, one of these two equal possibilities would have to be preferred without a cause. Since this is impossible, a cause must exist.
The conclusion is implicit: the impossibility of preference without cause proves the necessity of a creator.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لَزِمَ أَنْ يَكُونَ أَحَدُ الْأَمْرَيْنِ الْمُتَسَاوِيَيْنِ أي: اللذين هما: الوجود والعدمُ، والمراد بأحدهما الوجود، والمراد بصاحبه العَدَمُ، وهذا ـ كما ترى - مبني على أن الوجود والعدم بالنظر إلى ذات الممكن سيان، وهو المشهور.
وقيل : العَدَمُ راجح لأسبقيته، واللازم على هذا القول: لو لم يكن للعالم مُحْدِثٌ، بل حَدَثَ بنفسه؛ ترجّحَ المرجوحُ بلا سبب وهو أقوى في الاستحالة من اللازم على القول الأول.
(قوله: وَهُوَ مُحَالٌ) أي: لما فيه من اجتماع الرُّجْحَان والمساواة، وهما ضدان، ونظيرُ ذلك: ميزان اعتدلَتْ كفتاه ورَجَحَتْ إحداهما على الأخرى بلا سبب.
(قوله: أَيْ: اللذين هُمَا الْوُجُودُ وَالْعَدَمُ وقيل: المراد بهما طرفا الممكن اللذان هما الوجود والعدم، والمقدار المخصوص ومقابله من المقادير، والزمان المخصوص ومقابله من الأزمان إلى آخر الممكنات المتقابلات.
It is necessary that one of the two equal things [be preferred] – meaning: the two which are existence and non-existence, and what is meant by one of them is existence, and what is meant by its companion is non-existence. And this, as you see, is based on the fact that existence and non-existence with respect to the essence of the contingent are equal, and this is the well-known view. And it was said: non-existence is preponderant due to its priority, and the necessary conclusion on this statement: if the world had no originator, but rather it originated by itself, then the weaker [possibility] would have been preferred without a cause, and this is stronger in impossibility than the necessary conclusion on the first statement. (His saying: And it is impossible) means: because of the combination of preponderance and equality in it, and they are two opposites. The analogy of that is a balance whose two pans are equal, and one of them tips over the other without a cause. (His saying: meaning: the two which are existence and non-existence, and it was said: what is meant by them are the two extremities of the contingent which are existence and non-existence, and the specific quantity and its opposite among quantities, and the specific time and its opposite among times, up to the rest of the opposites of possibles.)
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of the argument from the impossibility of preference without a cause. It begins by stating that if the world had no originator, one of two equal possibilities (existence and non-existence) would have to be preferred, which is impossible. The commentator explains that the two equal possibilities are existence and non-existence, and that according to the well-known view, existence and non-existence are equal with respect to the essence of the contingent. It then presents an alternative view that non-existence is preferred due to its priority (because non-existence precedes existence). On this view, if the world had no originator, the weaker possibility (existence) would be preferred without cause, which is even more impossible. Then it explains that the impossibility is due to the contradiction between preference and equality. An analogy is given of a balance with equal pans, one of which tips without cause, which is impossible. Finally, a commentary by al-Ujhuri expands on the two equal things: they are not only existence and non-existence but also specific quantity and its opposite, specific time and its opposite, and all pairs of opposites in contingent things.

Key Terms
الوجود
العدم
الممكن
المتساويان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that existence and non-existence are not equal; existence is inherently better.
Correction: The argument assumes equality for the contingent before a cause; the well-known view is that they are equal in possibility.

Checkpoint
According to the well-known view, what is the relationship between existence and non-existence with respect to the essence of a contingent being?
A) Existence is more likely than non-existence.
B) They are equal.
C) Non-existence is preponderant.
D) They are impossible.

Key Takeaway
Existence and non-existence are equal for a contingent being, so a cause is needed to prefer one over the other.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Contemplate the fact that your own existence is not necessary; you could have just as easily not been born. What does this realization demand of you regarding your relationship with the One who preferred your existence over non-existence?
?
If everything in your life - your health, wealth, abilities - is contingent and could have been otherwise, why do you often take them for granted and act as if they are self-sustaining?
?
The passage states that existence and non-existence are equal for a contingent being. How does this truth challenge your sense of self-sufficiency and remind you of your constant need for the Creator's preservation?

The Alternative View and the Analogy of the Balance
Al-Bajuri presents an alternative view and the analogy of the balance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the stronger impossibility under the alternative view that non-existence is preponderant, and the analogy of the balance that illustrates the impossibility of preference without a cause.

Core Idea
Even if non-existence is preponderant, the existence of the world would be a preference without cause, which is even more impossible.

This lesson examines an alternative view that non-existence is preponderant due to its priority, and shows that even on this view, the existence of the world would require a preference without cause. Al-Bajuri then provides a vivid analogy of a balance to illustrate the impossibility of such a preference.

Guided Path
1
Read the alternative view
Read the Arabic passage starting from 'Wa qeela' and its translation. Understand that some scholars say non-existence is preponderant because it is prior.
Priority means non-existence is the default state before existence.
2
Analyze the stronger impossibility
On this alternative view, if the world had no originator, the weaker possibility (existence) would have to be preferred without cause, which is an even stronger impossibility.
3
Understand the analogy of the balance
Al-Bajuri gives an example of a balance with equal pans; if one pan tips without any cause, that is impossible. This illustrates the logical problem.
The balance analogy makes abstract logic tangible.
4
Note the expansion of the two equal things
The passage concludes by noting that the two equal things include not only existence and non-existence but also other pairs like specific quantity and its opposite, etc.
This shows the argument is general.

The Syllogism for the Origination of Bodies
Al-Bajuri explains the syllogism proving bodies originated via accidents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the kalam syllogism that proves bodies are originated because they are inseparable from originated accidents, and how the minor and major premises function.

Core Idea
Bodies are proved originated by their inseparability from changing accidents.

The kalam tradition builds its proof for God's existence on the origination of the universe. A key step is showing that bodies, which make up the universe, are not eternal. Al-Bajuri unpacks the syllogism that links bodies to accidents, themselves clearly originated, to reach this conclusion. This passage shows how the argument is structured.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scope of 'world'
Notice that Al-Bajuri clarifies that 'world' here means bodies only, not the general sense that includes accidents. This precision is important for the argument.
In earlier discussions, 'world' included both bodies and accidents.
2
Understand the minor premise
The minor premise states: bodies are inseparable (ملازمة) from originated accidents. This premise is based on observation—accidents like motion and rest are seen to change, and bodies always have some accident.
The phrase 'في قوة الصغرى' indicates this is the content of the minor premise.
3
Understand the major premise
The major premise states: everything inseparable from an originated thing is itself originated. This is a logical principle: if something never exists without something that is originated, it too must be originated.
Al-Bajuri explains this premise using the phrase 'في قوة الكبرى'.
4
Recognize the conclusion
From the two premises, the conclusion follows: bodies are originated. This is the intended proof for the origination of the world (bodies).
The conclusion is explicitly stated as 'ونتجته: الأجرام حادثة'.
5
Reflect on al-Anbabi's note
Muhammad al-Anbabi adds a remark about the word 'مُحدِث' (originator). He clarifies that if 'originator' means something other than itself, the argument is straightforward. If it includes itself, then the transition from general to specific is for clarification, not restriction.
This note helps avoid misunderstanding about the scope of the term.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: وَدَلِيلُ حُدُوثِ الْعَالَمِ... إلخ) قد عرفت أن هذا دليل على حدوث الأجرام، فالمراد من العالم هنا خصوص الأجرام، بخلافه فيما تقدم، فإن المراد به: ما يَشْمَلُ الأجرام والأعراض.
(قوله: مُلَازَمَتُهُ لِلْأَعْرَاضِ الْحَادِثَةِ في قوَّة الصغرى القائلة: الأجرامُ ملازِمَةٌ للأعراض الحادثة.
(وقوله: وَمُلَازِمُ الْحَادِثِ حَادِثٌ في قوَّة الكبرى القائلة: وكلُّ ما لازَمَ الحادث حادث، فيصير نَظْمُ الدليل هكذا الأجرام ملازمةٌ للأعراض الحادثة، وكلُّ ما لازم الحادث حادث، ونتيجته: الأجرام حادثة.
تقريرات العلامة محمد الأنبايي.
منه: مُحْدِثُ غير نفسه، فإن جَرَيْنا على أن المحدِثَ شامل لنفسه؛ فلا انتقال من الأعم إلى الأخضِ، بل الانتقال لأجل الإيضاح والتفسير. تأمل.
(His saying: And the proof of the occurrence of the world... etc.) You have known that this is a proof for the occurrence of bodies, so what is meant by 'world' here is specifically bodies, unlike in what preceded, for what is meant by it there is what includes bodies and accidents. (His saying: Its inseparability from originated accidents is in the strength of the minor premise which says: bodies are inseparable from originated accidents. (And His saying: And whatever is inseparable from an originated thing is originated is in the strength of the major premise which says: And everything that is inseparable from an originated thing is originated. So the arrangement of the proof becomes: bodies are inseparable from originated accidents, and everything that is inseparable from an originated thing is originated, and its conclusion: bodies are originated. Comments of the scholar Muhammad al-Anbabi. From it: 'Originator' is other than itself, for if we proceed on the basis that the originator includes itself, then there is no transition from the general to the specific, but rather the transition is for clarification and interpretation. Reflect.
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage presents the proof for the occurrence of bodies. It first clarifies that in this context, 'world' means bodies only, not the general sense that includes accidents. The proof is a syllogism with two premises: the minor premise states that bodies are always linked (ملازمة) to originated accidents; the major premise states that anything that is always linked to an originated thing is itself originated. The conclusion is that bodies are originated. This is a classic kalam argument. Then a note by Muhammad al-Anbabi comments on the term 'محدث' (originator) used in the proof. He explains that if we understand 'originator' to mean something other than the thing itself, then the argument is valid. If we extend its meaning to include the thing itself, then the transition from the general statement to the specific is not for restriction but for clarification.

Key Terms
الأجرام
الأعراض
الملازمة
الحدوث
الصغرى
الكبرى
المحدث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think the proof is circular because it assumes accidents are originated without proof.
Correction: The proof does not attempt to prove that accidents are originated; that is empirically evident (we see them change). The syllogism only uses this established fact to infer the origination of bodies.

Checkpoint
What is the minor premise in the proof for the origination of bodies?
A) Bodies are inseparable from originated accidents.
B) Everything inseparable from an originated thing is originated.
C) Bodies are originated.
D) Accidents are observed to change.

Key Takeaway
The proof for the origination of bodies is a valid syllogism: bodies are inseparable from originated accidents, and whatever is inseparable from an originated thing is itself originated, therefore bodies are originated.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have read a logical proof that every body around you is originated and dependent on a Creator. Does this truth sit in your mind as distant theory, or does it press on your heart in this very moment? What would change in your daily life if you truly felt that every atom is held in existence by God’s will?
?
The proof hinges on the fact that bodies are inseparable from changing accidents. When you witness change in your own body—aging, illness, or even a passing mood—do you instinctively see it as a sign of your dependence on the Creator, or do you attribute it to mere natural causes? How can you train yourself to read every change as a divine reminder?
?
Al-Bajuri carefully distinguishes what 'world' means in different contexts. Have you been guilty of using Islamic terms carelessly, assuming you know their meaning without precision? Are you willing to examine your understanding of key terms like 'world' and 'originated' in light of what you have learned, and let that precision reshape your faith?

Motion and Rest: Definitions and Their Nature as Accidents
Al-Bajuri defines motion and rest and discusses their status as accidents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why motion and rest are considered mental considerations (اعتباري) rather than existential accidents, and how they are defined in Islamic theology.

Core Idea
Motion and rest are mental considerations, not existential accidents.

In this passage, al-Bajuri clarifies why motion and rest are specifically mentioned in the proof for bodies. He then addresses a subtle point: although motion and rest are inseparable from bodies, they are not true accidents in the existential sense. This lesson unpacks the definitions and the theological reasoning behind their classification.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the literal translation to get the gist of the definitions and the discussion.
Notice the debate about motion and rest being 'اعتباري' (mental).
2
Identify the two definitions of motion
The passage gives two definitions: one as 'transition from place to place' and another as 'first occurrence in a different place'. Note how each definition pairs with its opposite, rest.
Both definitions frame motion and rest as relative, not absolute.
3
Understand why motion and rest are not existential accidents
Accidents (أعراض) are defined as existential qualities like blackness. Since motion and rest are relational (mental considerations), they do not fit the definition. This is a key nuance in kalam.
4
Reflect on the theological significance
Ask yourself: why does it matter that motion and rest are mental? It affects how we reason about bodies and their properties – they are not independently existing entities.
This connects to the broader proof for the createdness of bodies.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
:(قوله)) مِنْ حَرَكَةٍ وَسُكُونٍ وَغَيْرِهِمَا) بيان للأعراض الحادثة، وإنما خَصَّ الحركة والسكونَ بالتصريح بهما؛ لأن ملازمة الأجرام لهما ضرورية لكلّ عاقل، لكن في جَعْلِهما من الأعراض نَظَرٌ؛ لأن الأعراضَ جمع عَرَضٍ، وهو خاص بالأمر الوجودي كالسَّوَاد والبياض، ولا كذلك هما؛ لأن الحركة هي انتقالُ الجِرْمِ من حيّز إلى حيّز آخَرَ، والسكونَ ضده. وقيل الحركة: هي الحصول الأول في غير الحيّز الأول، والسكونُ ما عدا ذلك. وكلّ من الانتقال وضدّه، أو الحصولِ الأول في غير الحيّز الأول وما عداه أمرٌ اعتباري
(قوله: وَمُلَازِمُ الْحَادِثِ حَادِثٌ أي: لأن ملازِمَ الشيء لا يَصِحُ أن يسبقَهُ؛ إذ لو سَبَقَهُ لانتفت الملازمةُ ، وهو خلاف الغَرَضِ.
(قوله: وَدَلِيلُ حُدُوثِ الْأَعْرَاضِ مُشَاهَدَةُ تَغَيرِهَا... إلخ) تقريره هكذا: الأعراضُ شُوهِدَ تغيرها من وجود إلى عَدَمٍ وعكسه، وكل ما كان كذلك فهو حادث، ونتيجته الأعراض حادثة.
فإن قيل: التغيرُ أمر اعتباريّ لا تتعلَّق به المشاهَدَة؛ لأنها لا تتعلَّق إلا بالأمر الوجودي. أجيب بأن في العبارة تساهلاً والمراد أن الأعراض شوهِدَتْ متغيّرَةً من حاشية العلامة أحمد الأجهوري
(قوله: مِنْ حَرَكَةٍ وَسُكُونِ ولا يرد على ذلك أن من الأجرام ما هو متحرك دائماً وما هو ساكن دائماً كالجبال، وأن منها ما لا يعلم حاله كالأجرام التي تحت الأرض؛ لأن المراد بالحركة والسكون ما يشمل الحاصلين بالفعل والحاصلين بالقوة، وما ذكر لا يخرج عنهما بعد كون المراد ما ذكر
(قوله: مُشَاهَدَةُ (تَغَيْرِهَا ولا يقال: إن التغير هو الحدوث، فلا الاستدلال به يصح عليه؛ لأن الدليل مشاهدة التغير لا نفسه على أنا لا نسلم أن التغير هو الحدوث؛ لأن الحدوث هو الوجود بعد عدم والتغير هو الانتقال من عدم إلى وجود، ومن وجود إلى عدم، والجواب الأول أولى.
(His saying: From motion and rest and others) is an explanation of the originated accidents. And he specifically mentioned motion and rest explicitly because the inseparability of bodies from them is necessary for every rational person. However, there is consideration in making them accidents, because accidents are the plural of 'accident' (عَرَض), which is specific to an existent matter like blackness and whiteness, and they are not like that; because motion is the transition of a body from one place to another, and rest is its opposite. And it was said: motion is the first occurrence in a place other than the first place, and rest is other than that. And both transition and its opposite, or the first occurrence in a place other than the first and what is other than it, is a mental consideration. (His saying: And the inseparable from an originated thing is originated) means: because the inseparable from a thing cannot precede it; for if it preceded it, the inseparability would be negated, which is contrary to the purpose. (His saying: And the proof of the occurrence of accidents is the observation of their change... etc.) Its exposition is thus: Accidents are observed to change from existence to non-existence and vice versa, and everything that is like that is originated, and its conclusion: accidents are originated. For if it is said: change is a mental consideration to which observation does not pertain, because observation only pertains to an existent matter. The answer is that in the expression there is leniency, and the meaning is that accidents are observed to be changing. From the gloss of the scholar Ahmad al-Ujhuri: (His saying: From motion and rest) and it is not objected that among bodies are those that are always moving and those that are always still like mountains, and among them are those whose state is not known like bodies under the earth; because what is meant by motion and rest includes those that are actual and those that are potential, and what was mentioned does not exclude them after the meaning is what was mentioned. (His saying: Observation of their change) and it is not said that change is occurrence, so the inference by it is not valid; because the proof is the observation of change itself, and we do not concede that change is occurrence; because occurrence is existence after non-existence, while change is the transition from non-existence to existence and from existence to non-existence, and the first answer is more appropriate.
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage first clarifies that in the proof for bodies, motion and rest are specifically mentioned because their link to bodies is obvious. However, there is a consideration whether motion and rest are truly accidents, since accidents are defined as existent qualities like blackness and whiteness, whereas motion and rest are mental considerations (اعتباري). Two definitions of motion are given: 1) the transition of a body from one place to another; rest is its opposite. 2) motion is the first occurrence in a different place; rest is other than that. Both are mental. Then the proof for the occurrence of accidents is given: accidents are observed to change from existence to nonexistence and vice versa; whatever changes is originated. An objection arises that change is a mental consideration not observable. The answer is that the wording is lenient; the observation is actually of the accidents themselves changing. Then al-Ujhuri's commentary addresses two points: first, that bodies that are always moving or always still do not refute the proof because motion and rest include potential as well as actual. Second, that change should not be equated with occurrence; change is the transition between existence and nonexistence, while occurrence is existence after nonexistence. So the proof from observation is valid.

Key Terms
الحركة
السكون
الأعراض
اعتباري
الحيّز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many think motion and rest are real existential accidents like color.
Correction: The passage clarifies they are mental considerations (اعتباري), not existential qualities, because they are relational and not independently observable.

Checkpoint
Why is there doubt about classifying motion and rest as accidents?
A) Because they are inseparable from bodies.
B) Because accidents must be existential, but motion and rest are mental considerations.
C) Because motion and rest change over time.
D) Because the author did not mention them explicitly.

Key Takeaway
Motion and rest are mental considerations, not existential accidents, and they are defined relationally in terms of place and transition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have learned that motion and rest are mere mental considerations, not actual things. Yet you live your day assuming that 'movement' and 'stillness' are real properties of objects. How does this lesson challenge your default perception of reality? Will you now pause to reflect on what else you might be misinterpreting as real?
?
If motion is not an accident but a relational concept, then every physical action you perform—walking, speaking, raising your hand—is ultimately dependent on the Creator's power to create that relation moment by moment. Does your heart feel that dependence, or have you been acting as if your own movements arise from your own power?
?
The lesson distinguishes between mental considerations (اعتباري) and existential attributes. Think of an attribute you have always considered 'part of you'—perhaps your intelligence, your anger, or your kindness. If these are also mental considerations that depend on God's constant creation, what does that mean for your pride or your despair? Are you willing to see yourself as nothing but a succession of mental considerations held in existence by God?

The Proof for the Origination of Accidents from Observed Change
Al-Bajuri presents the proof for accidents from observed change and answers objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational proof for the origination of accidents based on observed change, and how objections about change being mental are answered.

Core Idea
Change is observable and proves that accidents are originated.

This lesson presents the core proof for the origination of accidents: we observe accidents changing from existence to non-existence and vice versa. Whatever changes is originated. Al-Bajuri then addresses an important objection: change is a mental consideration, so how can observation prove anything? He also clarifies the difference between change and origination.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof and its objection
Read the Arabic passage that states the proof (الأعراض شُوهِدَ تغيرها... إلخ) and the objection that change is a mental consideration. Then read the translation.
Notice the structure: proof, objection, answer, then two clarifications from al-Ujhuri.
2
Analyze the logical structure
The proof is a syllogism: (1) Accidents are observed to change (major premise). (2) Whatever changes is originated (minor premise). (3) Conclusion: Accidents are originated. Identify each part.
The minor premise is implied: 'كل ما كان كذلك فهو حادث'.
3
Understand the objection and answer
The objector says change is mental, so observation cannot apply. The answer explains that the wording is lenient; we actually observe the accidents themselves changing, not the abstract concept of change.
This defends the validity of empirical observation in theology.
4
Differentiate change from origination
Al-Ujhuri clarifies that change (transition between existence and non-existence) is not the same as origination (existence after non-existence). The proof relies on change, not origination, so the objection that change equals origination is invalid.
This nuance protects the proof from circular reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That change and origination are the same thing, so the proof is circular.
Correction: Change is transition between existence and non-existence; origination is existence after non-existence. They are distinct, and the proof relies on change, not origination.

Checkpoint
How does al-Bajuri answer the objection that change is a mental consideration not observable?
A) He denies that change is mental.
B) He says the wording is lenient; we actually observe accidents changing.
C) He changes the definition of change.
D) He says observation is not needed.

Key Takeaway
Accidents are proven to be originated because they are observed to change, and change is not the same as origination but implies it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you observe the constant changes in your own body, thoughts, and emotions, do you ever stop to consider that these are not self-caused? How does the fact that accidents are originated lead you to acknowledge a Creator who sustains you every moment? Commit today to pause during a moment of change (like feeling tired or happy) and mentally attribute it to Allah.
?
The objection says change is a mental concept not observable. But your own life is full of tangible changes. Are you tempted to intellectualize away the clear signs of Allah's power? In what area of your life have you been treating change as just a mental abstraction rather than a real call to worship? Write down one specific change you have been ignoring as a sign from Allah.
?
The proof relies on observation of change. Yet you often go through days without noticing the countless changes happening in you and around you. Does your heedlessness of these changes reflect a heedlessness of the One who brings them about? What will you do today to become more aware of the constant renewal of creation as a reminder of the Creator?

The Principle: The Concomitant of the Temporal is Temporal
Al-Bajuri explains the principle that whatever is concomitant with a temporal thing must itself be temporal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why, according to the principle of concomitance, whatever is concomitant with a temporal thing must itself be temporal, and how this principle supports arguments for the temporality of the world.

Core Idea
If something always accompanies a temporal thing, it cannot be eternal.

This short but powerful principle is a cornerstone of kalam arguments for the world's creation. Al-Bajuri explains the logical reasoning: if the concomitant of a temporal thing were eternal and preceded it, then at that time the concomitant would exist without the temporal thing, breaking the necessary connection. This lesson unpacks that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the concise wording.
This is a complete argument in one sentence.
2
Identify the key terms
Find the words ملازم (concomitant), حادث (temporal), سبق (precede), and ملازمة (concomitance). Understand their meanings.
These are technical kalam terms.
3
Follow the logical reasoning
Al-Bajuri argues: if the concomitant preceded, the concomitance would be negated. Why? Because the temporal thing would exist without its concomitant at that earlier time.
This shows that the concomitant cannot be eternal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: وَمُلَازِمُ الْحَادِثِ حَادِثٌ أي: لأن ملازِمَ الشيء لا يَصِحُ أن يسبقَهُ؛ إذ لو سَبَقَهُ لانتفت الملازمةُ ، وهو خلاف الغَرَضِ.
His saying: 'And the concomitant of the temporal is temporal' means: because the concomitant of a thing cannot validly precede it; for if it preceded it, the concomitance would be negated, which contradicts the purpose.
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This is a brief but crucial principle in kalam used to prove the temporality of the world. Al-Bajuri explains that if something is concomitant (mulazim) to a temporal thing (hadith), then the concomitant itself must be temporal too. The reasoning is that if the concomitant were eternal (qadim) and preceded the temporal thing, then the temporal thing would be without its concomitant at some time, meaning the concomitance is not necessary. The purpose (gharad) of asserting concomitance is to show that they always exist together. So if the concomitant preceded, that purpose fails. Therefore, the concomitant must also be temporal.

Key Terms
ملازم
حادث
ملازمة
سبق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The concomitant of a temporal thing can be eternal even if the temporal thing is not.
Correction: The principle shows that the concomitant must also be temporal because if it preceded, the necessary concomitance would break.

Checkpoint
According to al-Bajuri, why must the concomitant of a temporal thing also be temporal?
A) Because it is an accident.
B) Because if it preceded, the concomitance would be negated.
C) Because it is observed to change.
D) Because the author said so.

Key Takeaway
The concomitant of a temporal thing must be temporal; otherwise, the necessary co-existence of the two would be violated.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The principle shows that if something is temporal, everything necessarily connected to it is also temporal. Your soul is temporal—it had a beginning. Do you live as if your knowledge and will are eternal, or do you acknowledge their temporal nature and seek guidance from the Eternal?
?
If you treat any temporal thing (your wealth, status, relationships) as though it will last forever, you are implicitly denying this principle—that its temporal nature means it cannot be permanent. In what specific area of your life are you most attached to something temporal, and how can you let go of that illusion today?
